Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Introducing the Author and Title of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq
Title page introduces the book and author of this logic work.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author and full title of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq and explain its place in the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq is a high-level logic text by one of Islam's greatest theologian-logicians, al-Amidi.

Before diving into the logical content of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, it is essential to know who wrote it and why. This lesson covers the title page, giving you the author's full name and his significance in Islamic intellectual history. Understanding the author's background prepares you to engage with the text's sophisticated arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic title and translation
Examine the Arabic text: 'دَقَائِق الحقائق قسم علم المنطق'. The title 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' means 'Subtleties of Realities'. The phrase 'قسم علم المنطق' indicates it is the section on logic. This tells you the book's subject matter.
The title suggests the work deals with precise, nuanced truths in logic.
2
Identify the author's name
Locate 'أبي الحسد علي بن أبي علي الآمدي (سيف الدين الآمدي)'. Note the likely scribal error: 'الحسد' should be 'الحسن' (al-Hasan). The author is Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Abi Ali al-Amidi, known as Sayf al-Din al-Amidi (d. 631 AH).
Al-Amidi was a prominent Ash'ari theologian and logician.
3
Recognize the editor and publication details
The line 'ضبط وتصحيح السيّد فَاضِل علي الموسوي النجف الأشرق' identifies the editor and location: al-Sayyid Fadil Ali al-Musawi in Najaf al-Ashraf. This shows the text's scholarly lineage.
Najaf is a historic center of Islamic learning.
4
Reflect on the author's significance
Al-Amidi wrote major works in logic and kalam, such as 'Abkar al-Afkar'. His logical works are studied for their rigorous synthesis of Aristotelian logic with Islamic theology.
He is known for his critical engagement with falsafa and his defense of Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دَقَائِق الحقائق قسم علم المنطق تأليف أبي الحسد علي بن أبي علي الآمدي (سيف الدين الآمدي) ضبط وتصحيح السيّد فَاضِل علي الموسوي النجف الأشرق ١-٢ دقائق الحقائق قسم علم المنطق
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, Section on the Science of Logic. Authored by Abu al-Hasan Ali bin Abi Ali al-Amidi (Sayf al-Din al-Amidi). Edited and corrected by al-Sayyid Fadil Ali al-Musawi. Najaf al-Ashraf. 1-2. Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, Section on the Science of Logic.
Source pages p. 1

Commentary
This passage is the title page of the book 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' (The Subtleties of Realities) which is a work on the science of logic (ilm al-mantiq). The author is Abu al-Hasan Ali bin Abi Ali al-Amidi, commonly known as Sayf al-Din al-Amidi (d. 631 AH/1233 CE). He was a prominent Ash'ari theologian and logician whose works in logic and kalam are highly respected. The title indicates that the book deals with precise logical subtleties. The editor is al-Sayyid Fadil Ali al-Musawi, and the publication location is Najaf al-Ashraf, a major center of Islamic learning. This front matter provides essential bibliographic context for the student beginning the study of this text.

Key Terms
دقائق الحقائق
علم المنطق
سيف الدين الآمدي

Checkpoint
Who is the author of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq?
A) Abu Hamid al-Ghazali
B) Abu al-Hasan al-Amidi
C) Abu Nasr al-Farabi
D) Abu Ali Ibn Sina

Key Takeaway
The front matter of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq identifies it as a work on logic by the renowned Ash'ari scholar Sayf al-Din al-Amidi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look up the entry for Sayf al-Din al-Amidi in a biographical dictionary (e.g., Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya). Write one sentence about his major contributions to logic or theology.
?
Examine the title page of another classical text (e.g., al-Ghazali's Mantiq al-Tahafut). Identify the author, title, and publication details.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Book Title and Subject Classification
Author names the book and identifies it as a work on logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject classification of a classical Islamic text from its title and subtitle.

Core Idea
A book's title and section heading tell you immediately whether it belongs to logic, fiqh, kalam, or other sciences.

When you open a classical Islamic manuscript, the first thing you see is its title. The title often includes a subject classification. In this lesson, we examine the opening of 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq', which explicitly declares itself a work on logic ('ilm al-mantiq). Recognizing such classifications helps you instantly place a text within the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic title
Notice the words: دقائق الحقائق and the phrase قسم علم المنطق. The author names the book and then defines its field.
2
Translate the phrase
'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' means 'Subtleties of Truths'. 'Qism ilm al-mantiq' means 'section on logic'.
3
Classify the subject
Based on the phrase, we classify this book as a work on logic. This is important because logic (mantiq) is the foundation of reasoning in Islamic scholarship.
4
Memorize the title and subject
Commit to memory: the book is a logic text. This anchor will help you recall the content when you hear the title.
This step is a memorization anchor for future reference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دقائق الحقائق قسم علم المنطق
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: section on logic.
Source pages p. 2

Commentary
This passage names the work and identifies its subject. 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' translates to 'Subtleties of Truths'. The author states that this book belongs to the field of logic ('ilm al-mantiq). This is front matter serving as the book's title and classification.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

What is the subject of the book Daqaiq al-Haqaiq?
A) Physics
B) Logic
C) Metaphysics
D) Grammar

Key Takeaway
The title 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' together with the phrase 'section on logic' identifies this work as a textbook on the science of logic, foundational for Islamic reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the following titles and classify each as belonging to logic, fiqh, or kalam: (1) 'Al-Muqaddimat al-Arba' fi 'Ilm al-Mantiq', (2) 'Al-Waraqat fi Usul al-Fiqh', (3) 'Al-Iqtisad fi al-I'tiqad'.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Introduction to Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Front Matter and Author
Front matter including title, author, and dedication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author, title, and significance of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq from its front matter and explain why such prefatory material is important in Islamic scholarly works.

Core Idea
A book's front matter is a gateway to its author's authority and the work's intellectual lineage.

This lesson introduces the front matter of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, a major work in Islamic logic and theology by Sayfuddin al-Amidi. Although seemingly introductory, the titles and dedication give us insight into the author's stature and the purpose of the book. Understanding these details anchors our study and reminds us that Islamic scholarship values both content and the chain of transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the title and its meaning
The title 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' means 'Subtleties of Realities' or 'Fine Points of Truths'. The term 'haqaiq' (realities/truths) is central to Islamic philosophy and logic, indicating the book's focus on deep truths.
The title itself signals that this work deals with precise, refined issues in logic and metaphysics.
2
Identify the author's full name and death date
The author is Sayfuddin Ali ibn Abi Ali al-Amidi, who died in 631 AH. His name includes his nisba (al-Amidi), linking him to the city of Amid. The death date helps situate him in the timeline of Islamic thought.
Knowing the death date is crucial for understanding the historical context and the chain of transmission (sanad) of knowledge.
3
Analyze the eulogistic titles
The passage praises al-Amidi as 'king of scholars', 'sultan of theologians', etc. Such titles reflect his high status in the eyes of later scholars and indicate that his work was highly regarded.
These titles are not mere flattery; they serve as a recommendation of the author's authority and reliability.
4
Understand the function of front matter
In classical Islamic works, the front matter establishes the author's credentials, states the work's title and scope, and often includes prayers for the author. This helps the reader assess the work's value and authenticity.
This practice is common across all Islamic sciences, from hadith to fiqh to kalam.

Source Text

دقائق الحقائق
سيف الدين علي بن أبي علي الآمدي
ت ٦٣١ هـ
المجلد الأول
من كتاب دقائق الحقائق
مصنف سيدنا ومولانا الإمام العالم ملك العلماء، أوحد الدين سيد الدنيا والدين سلطان المتكلمين سيد الفضلاء، أفضل المتأخرين أبي الحسن علي ابن أبي علي الآمدي أطال الله عمره كما جمل وجهه آمين
Subtleties of Realities. Sayfuddin Ali ibn Abi Ali al-Amidi (died 631 AH). Volume One. From the book 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq'. Authored by our master and lord, the Imam, the scholar, the king of scholars, the unique of the religion, the master of the world and the religion, the sultan of theologians, the master of the virtuous, the most excellent of the later ones, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Abi Ali al-Amidi — may God prolong his life as He has beautified his face, amen.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage is the front matter of the book 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' by Sayfuddin Ali ibn Abi Ali al-Amidi (d. 631 AH). It gives the title, the author's name, his death date (631 AH), and a dedication. The author is described with lofty titles such as 'king of scholars', 'sultan of theologians', etc. This is typical of premodern Islamic scholarly works, where the author is praised in a eulogistic manner. The phrase 'Subtleties of Realities' indicates the book's focus on deep logical and philosophical truths.

Key Terms

Who is the author of 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq'?
A) Fakhr al-Din al-Razi
B) Sayfuddin al-Amidi

D) al-Ghazali

Key Takeaway
The front matter of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq introduces al-Amidi as a towering figure in Islamic logic and theology, and the title hints at the work's focus on deep truths (haqaiq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find the front matter of another classical Islamic text (e.g., al-Ghazali's Ihya' Ulum al-Din or Ibn Sina's al-Shifa) and identify the author, title, and any eulogistic phrases. Write a short paragraph on what this front matter tells you about the work's significance.
?
Look up the biography (tarjama) of Sayfuddin al-Amidi in a biographical dictionary like Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya. Compare the titles given in the front matter with those in his biography.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Need for Logic
Diagnosis of the need for logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reason why the science of logic was developed, as stated by al-Amidi.

Core Idea
Logic is born from the mind's struggle to achieve consistency in thought.

This opening passage doesn't just praise; it diagnoses a fundamental problem: the human mind often fails to harmonize its concepts and actions, leading to inconsistency. The author presents logic as the cure—a set of laws and scales to bring agreement and compatibility. Let's examine how he frames this need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem described
Find the sentence where the author says the mind 'oscillates' or 'differs and fails in harmonization'. What exactly is the problem?
Look for words like 'يختلف' and 'يتخلف في المواءمة'.
2
Understand the consequence
The author says this leads to 'divergent outcomes' from one and the same idea. Why is that dangerous for knowledge?
Think about how this affects certainty in Islamic sciences.
3
Recognize the solution
The author states that rational minds sought a 'point of agreement' and 'criterion of compatibility'. How did they achieve this?
They 'normalized the principle of numbers' and formulated 'laws and scales'.
4
Define logic in this context
The passage defines logic as those 'laws and scales based on what should be'. How does this definition connect to the problem?
Logic is prescriptive, not descriptive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجلد الأول
من كتاب دقائق الحقائق
مصنف سيدنا ومولانا الإمام العالم ملك العلماء، أوحد الدين سيد الدنيا والدين سلطان المتكلمين سيد الفضلاء، أفضل المتأخرين أبي الحسن علي ابن أبي علي الآمدي أطال الله عمره كما جمل وجهه آمين

بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
مقدمة
الحمد لله الذي تعالى في شهوده ففاق نواظر الأبصار، والصلاة والسلام على شاهد مشاهد حقيقته محمد النبي في الجهر والإسرار، وعلى أهل بيته شواهد الأقدار، خزان علم العزيز الجبار.
قد يتأرجح العقل في عمليات تقدير المفاهيم للأفعال المترتبة على ما يكمن في حيثياتها، فيختلف ويتخلف في المواءمة حتى في الفكرة الواحدة نفسها، فَنُحَصل على ما يتباين منها، لكن يبقى الاستفهام حول انعدام الاتساق. كان ذلك المحور الذي سار في أفقه المتعقلون، للحصول على نقطة الاتفاق، ومعيار التوافق، فقام بعضهم بتطبيع مبدأ الأعداد على الفكرة للوصول إلى ذلك المرتكز، وصياغة قوانين وموازين على أساس ما ينبغي سُمي بالمنطق.
بدأ مع بداية التفكير الإنساني المنظم حينما قام الفيلسوف الإغريقي أرسطوطاليس (384 - 322ق.م) بتهذيب مسائله وفصوله، ووضع المجموعة الأولى لملفاته، والتي عُرفت بـ (الأورغانون) ضمت الكتب التالية: (كتاب المقولات – كتاب العبارة - كتاب التحليلات الأولى - كتاب التحليلات الثانية - كتاب الجدل - كتاب الفلسفة)، ويضاف إليها (كتاب الخطابة - كتاب الشعر) وترجمت كتب أرسطو في المنطق إلى العربية في القرن الثاني الهجري، وقيل في القرن الأول من قبل النقلة السريان وأشهرهم إسحاق بن حنين (ت 950م) وهو الذي ترجم كتاب المقولات.
ثم جاءت محاولة فرفريوس من أهالي مدينة صور الساحلية الواقعة في جنوب لبنان (233 - 304م) حينما وضع كتابه (إيساغوجي) وهي كلمة يونانية تعني
Volume One of the book Daqaiq Haqaiq. Authored by our master and lord, the imam, the scholar, the king of scholars, the unique of the religion, the master of the world and the religion, the sultan of theologians, the master of the virtuous, the best of the later scholars, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Abi Ali al-Amidi – may God prolong his life as He beautified his face. Amen. In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Introduction. Praise be to God, who is exalted in His witnessing, surpassing the sight of eyes, and prayer and peace upon the witness of the witnessed of His reality, Muhammad the Prophet in public and secret, and upon his family, the witnesses of destinies, the treasurers of the knowledge of the Almighty, the Compeller. The mind may oscillate in the operations of estimating concepts for actions based on what lies in their circumstances, thus differing and failing in harmonization even in one and the same idea, so we obtain divergent outcomes, but the question remains about the lack of consistency. That was the axis around which the rational minds revolved, to obtain a point of agreement and a criterion of compatibility, so some of them normalized the principle of numbers to the idea to reach that foundation, and formulated laws and scales based on what should be, called logic. It began with the start of organized human thought when the Greek philosopher Aristotle (384-322 BC) refined its issues and chapters, and placed the first collection of his works, known as the Organon, which included the following books: (Categories, On Interpretation, Prior Analytics, Posterior Analytics, Topics, and Sophistical Refutations), and added to them (Rhetoric and Poetics). Aristotle's books on logic were translated into Arabic in the second century AH, and it is said in the first century, by Syriac translators, the most famous of whom was Ishaq ibn Hunayn (d. 950 CE), who translated the Categories. Then came the attempt of Porphyry, from the city of Tyre on the coast of southern Lebanon (233-304 CE), when he wrote his book Eisagoge, which is a Greek word meaning.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the book 'Daqaiq Haqaiq'. It begins with praise of God and blessings upon the Prophet and his family, establishing the religious context. The author then introduces himself and his lineage, showing his authority in the field. The core of the passage is a historical introduction to the science of logic. It explains why logic is needed: because the mind often fails to harmonize concepts and actions, leading to inconsistency. To achieve agreement and a standard of compatibility, scholars formulated laws and measures, which they called 'logic'. The author traces the origin of logic to Aristotle, who systematized its problems and wrote the Organon, a collection of works including Categories, On Interpretation, Prior and Posterior Analytics, Topics, and Sophistical Refutations, with Rhetoric and Poetics sometimes added. He notes that Aristotle's logical works were translated into Arabic in the second century AH (8th century CE), possibly as early as the first, by Syriac translators like Ishaq ibn Hunayn. The passage ends by mentioning Porphyry, who wrote the Eisagoge (Introduction), a Greek word meaning 'introduction', which served as an introductory text to Aristotle's logic. This historical account situates the book within the broader tradition of logic, showing its origins and development. The author is positioning his work as a continuation of this tradition, aiming to provide a clear exposition of logical principles.

Key Terms
المنطق
الأورغانون
إيساغوجي
المقولات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what problem led to the development of logic?
A) The mind's tendency to forget
B) The mind's failure to harmonize concepts and actions consistently
C) The lack of books on reasoning
D) The influence of Greek philosophy

Key Takeaway
Logic was developed because the human mind naturally fails to achieve consistency in thought, and systematic laws are needed to ensure agreement and compatibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a situation in Islamic jurisprudence where two seemingly similar cases might lead to contradictory rulings if the reasoning is not harmonized. Describe how logic, as defined in the passage, would help achieve consistency.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Historical Development of Logic
Historical account of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the key figures and works in the early history of logic as presented in this passage.

Core Idea
From Aristotle's Organon to Porphyry's Eisagoge, logic traveled through Syriac translators into the Islamic world.

This passage provides a concise history of logic, beginning with Aristotle's systematic compilation and moving through the translation movement into Arabic. Let's unpack the chronological sequence and the key works mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first key figure
Who does the passage credit with refining the issues and writing the first collection? Note the name and the collection's title.
The collection is called 'Organon'.
2
List the books of the Organon
Find the list of books that the Organon included. How many are explicitly named? Are any additional books mentioned?
Look for the parentheses and the conjunction 'and'.
3
Examine the translation movement
When were Aristotle's logical works translated into Arabic? By whom? The passage mentions a specific translator—what did he translate?
Ishaq ibn Hunayn translated the Categories.
4
Identify Porphyry's contribution
Who is the next figure mentioned? What did he write, and what is the meaning of the title 'Eisagoge'?
The passage gives the Greek meaning as 'introduction'.

Authored by our master and lord, the imam, the scholar, the king of scholars, the unique of the religion, the master of the world and the religion, the sultan of theologians, the master of the virtuous, the best of the later scholars, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Abi Ali al-Amidi – may God prolong his life as He beautified his face. Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the book 'Daqaiq Haqaiq'. It begins with praise of God and blessings upon the Prophet and his family, establishing the religious context. The author then introduces himself and his lineage, showing his authority in the field. The core of the passage is a historical introduction to the science of logic. It explains why logic is needed: because the mind often fails to harmonize concepts and actions, leading to inconsistency. To achieve agreement and a standard of compatibility, scholars formulated laws and measures, which they called 'logic'. The author traces the origin of logic to Aristotle, who systematized its problems and wrote the Organon, a collection of works including Categories, On Interpretation, Prior and Posterior Analytics, Topics, and Sophistical Refutations, with Rhetoric and Poetics sometimes added. He notes that Aristotle's logical works were translated into Arabic in the second century AH (8th century CE), possibly as early as the first, by Syriac translators like Ishaq ibn Hunayn. The passage ends by mentioning Porphyry, who wrote the Eisagoge (Introduction), a Greek word meaning 'introduction', which served as an introductory text to Aristotle's logic. This historical account situates the book within the broader tradition of logic, showing its origins and development. The author is positioning his work as a continuation of this tradition, aiming to provide a clear exposition of logical principles.

Key Terms
المنطق
الأورغانون
إيساغوجي
المقولات

Key Takeaway
The history of logic in the Islamic world begins with the translation of Aristotle's Organon and Porphyry's Eisagoge, key foundational texts.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Origin and Purpose of Logic
Author recounts the origin of logic and Aristotle's Organon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic was formulated as a discipline and identify its foundational texts as described in the preface.

Core Idea
Logic was born from the quest for consistent reasoning across differing minds.

The author opens by reflecting on how the intellect can oscillate and produce conflicting ideas even on the same subject. He explains that this inconsistency led early thinkers to seek a universal standard for correct reasoning. This lesson unpacks that foundational motivation and the initial corpus—Aristotle's Organon—that was translated into Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem stated by the author
Find the sentence where the author describes intellectual inconsistency—'the intellect sometimes oscillates... fails in harmony even in the same idea.' This is the catalyst for logic.
2
Extract the proposed solution
The author says rational people moved to obtain a 'point of agreement and criterion of harmony.' They normalized principles onto ideas and formulated laws and measures called logic.
3
Locate Aristotle's role
The author states Aristotle 'refined its issues and chapters' and placed the first collection (the Organon). List the books mentioned: Categories, On Interpretation, Prior Analytics, Posterior Analytics, Topics, Sophistics, plus Rhetoric and Poetics.
These eight books are known as the 'eight heads' (al-ru'us al-thamaniya).
4
Trace the translation movement
The text says Aristotle's works were translated into Arabic in the second century AH (or first) by Syrian translators, most famously Ishaq ibn Hunayn, who translated the Categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمد لله الذي تعالى في شهودِه ففاقَ نواظر الأبصار، والصلاةُ والسَّلام على شاهد مشاهد حقيقته مُحمد النّبي في الجهر والإسرار، وعلى أهل بيته شواهد الأقدار، خُزّان علم العزيز الجبّار.
قد يتأرجح العقل في عمليات تصدير المفاهيم للأفعال المترتبة على ما يكمن في حيثياتها، فيختلف ويتخلف في المواءمة حتى في الفكرة الواحدة نفسها، فَنُحَصل على ما يتباين منها، لكن يبقى الاستفهام حول انعدام الاتساق. كان ذلك المحور الذي سار في أفقه المتعقلون، للحصول على نقطة الاتفاق، ومعيار التوافق، فقام بعضهم بتطبيع مبدأ الأعداد على الفكرة للوصول إلى ذلك المرتكز، وصياغة قوانين وموازين على أساس ما ينبغي سُمّي بالمنطق.
بدأ مع بداية التفكير الإنساني المنظم حينما قام الفيلسوف الإغريقي أرسطو طاليس (384 - 322ق.م) بتهذيب مسائله وفصوله، ووضع المجموعة الأولى لملفاته، والتي عُرفت بـ (الأورغان أورغانون ضمت الكتب التالية: (كتاب المقولات – كتاب العبارة - كتاب التحليلات الأولى - كتاب التحليلات الثانية - كتاب الجدل - كتاب الفلسفة)، ويضاف إليها (كتاب الخطابة - كتاب الشعر) وترجمت كتب أرسطو في المنطق إلى العربية في القرن الثاني الهجري، وقيل في القرن الأول من قبل النقلة السريان وأشهرهم إسحاق بن حنين ت) (950م) وهو الذي ترجم كتاب المقولات.
ثم جاءت محاولة فرفريوس من أهالي مدينة صور الساحلية الواقعة في جنوب لبنان 233 - 304ق.م) حينما وضع كتابه (إيساغوجي) وهي كلمة يونانية تعني (المدخل) وهو الاسم الثاني لهذا الكتاب؛ لأنه يبحث في الكليات الخمس، ونقله إلى العربية أبو عثمان الدمشقي في القرن التاسع الميلادي، واختصره أثير الدين المفضل بن عمر الأبهري (ت 663 هـ - (1264م) وكتبت عليه العديد من الشروح - والحواشي المختلفة، المختلفة، ثمّ جالينوس (ت سنة (160م الذي أضاف الشكل الرابع إلى الأشكال الثلاثة، وما كتب عليه من شروح، كان أهمها كتاب الشكوك لأبي زكريا الرازي، وكذا الحال عند فلاسفة الإسلام، حيث وضع العديد منهم والمؤلفات القيمة، كأبي نصر الفارابي (ت 950م) المُلقب بـ (المعلم الثاني)، والشيخ الرئيس ابن سينا، والقطب الرازي والخواجة نصير الدين الطوسي، والقاضي أبي الثناء الأرموي، والعلامة الأخضري، والعلامة الحلي، والسيد الشريف الجرجاني، وكثيرين غيرهم.
فحدث تطور كبير في موضوعاته، بعد أن توافرت الرؤوس الثمانية، فكان علماً مستقلاً بذاته. إن قلت: إن كانت موضوعات علم المنطق موازين عقليّة، فلِمَ الاختلاف؟ قلنا: يمكن جواب ذلك بمستويين: المستوى الأول: اختلاف الرؤى في بيان وشرح تلك القواعد، لا يعني تناقضها، بل تجري المحاولات المتعدّدة لإيصال تطبيقاتها إلى الأذهان، وهو ما يُعرف بالشروح والحواشي على تلك القواعد. المستوى الثاني: العقل البشري في حركة تطويرية مستمرة، فنحتاج إلى قواعد جديدة تتوافق والمرحلة المعاصرة له.
وما نقرّض له هو إحدى تلك المحاولات، جاءت في القرن الخامس الهجري، وضعها الفيلسوف المتكلم سيف الدين الآمدي (551 - 631هـ) تحت عنوان "دقائق الحقائق"، قال في مقدمتها فسألني من تعيّن إجابته، وتحتمت علي تلبيته، جمع کتاب جامع لدقائق الحقائق من العلوم الثلاثة وهي المنطقية، والطبيعية والإلهيّة، لكني لم أعثر إلا على ما يحتوي المباحث المنطقية، تناول فيها مباحث
Praise be to Allah who is exalted in His witnessing, surpassing the sights of eyes. Blessings and peace be upon the witness of the witnessed of His reality, Muhammad the Prophet in public and secret, and upon his family, witnesses of destinies, treasurers of the knowledge of the Mighty, the Compeller.
The intellect sometimes oscillates in the operations of exporting concepts for actions resulting from what lies in their circumstances, so it differs and fails in harmony even in the same idea. We obtain what differs from it, but the question about the lack of consistency remains. That was the axis around which rational people moved, to obtain a point of agreement and a criterion of harmony. So some of them normalized the principle of numbers onto the idea to reach that foundation, and formulated laws and measures based on what ought to be, which was called logic.
It began with the beginning of organized human thought when the Greek philosopher Aristotle (384-322 BC) refined its issues and chapters, and placed the first collection of his files, known as (the Organon), which included the following books: (Categories - On Interpretation - Prior Analytics - Posterior Analytics - Topics - Sophistics), and to them are added (Rhetoric - Poetics). Aristotle's books on logic were translated into Arabic in the second century AH, and it is said in the first century by the Syrian translators, the most famous of whom was Ishaq ibn Hunayn (d. 950 CE), who translated the Categories.
Then came the attempt of Porphyry, from the people of the coastal city of Tyre in southern Lebanon (233-304 BC), when he wrote his book (Isagoge), which is a Greek word meaning (Introduction), which is the second name of this book because it discusses the five universals. It was translated into Arabic by Abu Uthman al-Dimashqi in the ninth century AD, and abridged by Athir al-Din al-Mufaddal ibn Umar al-Abhari (d. 663 AH / 1264 CE). Many commentaries and glosses were written on it. Then Galen (d. 160 CE) added the fourth figure to the three figures. Among the commentaries written on it, the most important was the Book of Doubts by Abu Zakariyya al-Razi. Likewise the case among the philosophers of Islam, where many of them wrote valuable works, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi (d. 950 CE) nicknamed 'the Second Teacher', and the Chief Master Ibn Sina, and Qutb al-Din al-Razi, and Khwaja Nasir al-Din al-Tusi, and Qadi Abi al-Thana al-Urmawi, and al-Allama al-Akhdari, and al-Allama al-Hilli, and al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, and many others.
Then a great development occurred in its subjects, after the eight heads were available, so it became an independent science. If you say: Since the subjects of logic are rational measures, why the disagreement? We say: The answer to that can be at two levels. The first level: Differences in visions in explaining those rules do not imply contradiction; rather, multiple attempts occur to convey their applications to minds, which is known as commentaries and glosses on those rules. The second level: The human intellect is in continuous developmental movement, so we need new rules that accord with its contemporary stage.
What we are undertaking is one of those attempts, which came in the fifth century AH, written by the philosopher-theologian Sayf al-Din al-Amidi (551-631 AH) under the title 'Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq'. He said in its preface: 'One whom it was incumbent to answer asked me, and whose request was obligatory to fulfill, to compile a comprehensive book of the subtleties of truths from the three sciences, namely logic, physics, and theology, but I have only found that which contains the logical investigations.' He discussed in it investigations...
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is the preface (muqaddimah) of the book 'Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq'. It opens with praise of Allah, blessings on the Prophet and his family, then discusses the nature of intellectual disagreement and the origin of logic. The author explains that logic emerged as a tool to standardize thought and achieve consistency. He then provides a historical overview: Aristotle systematized logic with the Organon, translated into Arabic by Syriac scholars like Ishaq ibn Hunayn. Porphyry wrote the Isagoge (introduction) focusing on the five universals; al-Abhari later abridged it. Galen added the fourth figure of the syllogism. Islamic philosophers like al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and others further developed the field. The author notes that logic became an independent science after the 'eight heads' (the eight books of the Organon plus Rhetoric and Poetics). He then addresses a potential objection: if logic is rational, why is there disagreement? He answers on two levels: first, differences in explanation do not mean contradiction; second, human reason evolves, requiring new rules. Finally, he introduces his own work as a contribution in the fifth century AH by al-Amidi, but notes that only the logical portion of the planned three-part work (logic, physics, theology) has been found.

Key Terms
المنطق
الأورغانون
إسحاق بن حنين

Key Takeaway
Logic emerged as a response to the observed inconsistency of human thought, and Aristotle's Organon became its foundational corpus.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
12 min
The Transmission and Development of Logic
Author continues the historical overview of logic after Aristotle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the chain of transmission from Aristotle to Islamic logicians, identifying key figures like Porphyry, Galen, al-Farabi, and Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is built on Greek foundations but was significantly expanded and systematized by Muslim scholars.

After Aristotle, Porphyry wrote the Isagoge (Introduction), focusing on the five universals, and Galen added the fourth syllogistic figure. These works were translated and further developed by Islamic philosophers such as al-Farabi (the Second Teacher), Ibn Sina, al-Tusi, and others. This lesson guides you through that transmission chain as presented in the preface.

Guided Path
1
Review Porphyry's contribution
Find where the author mentions Porphyry (Farfuriyus) and his book Isagoge (Isaghuji). Note that it discusses the 'five universals' and was translated by Abu Uthman al-Dimashqi and later abbreviated by al-Abhari.
2
Identify Galen's addition
The author says Galen (Jalinus) added the 'fourth figure' to the three existing figures of the syllogism. Note this as a key expansion.
3
List the Islamic philosophers mentioned
Scan the text for names: al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, Qutb al-Din al-Razi, Nasir al-Din al-Tusi, al-Urmawi, al-Akhdari, al-Hilli, al-Jurjani. Understand that they wrote valuable works and commentaries.
Al-Farabi is called 'the Second Teacher' (al-mu'allim al-thani).
4
Understand the 'eight heads'
The author says that after the 'eight heads' (the eight books of logic: Organon plus Rhetoric and Poetics) were available, logic became an independent science. This marks its maturity.

Praise be to Allah who is exalted in His witnessing, surpassing the sights of eyes. Blessings and peace be upon the witness of the witnessed of His reality, Muhammad the Prophet in public and secret, and upon his family, witnesses of destinies, treasurers of the knowledge of the Mighty, the Compeller.
The intellect sometimes oscillates in the operations of exporting concepts for actions resulting from what lies in their circumstances, so it differs and fails in harmony even in the same idea. We obtain what differs from it, but the question about the lack of consistency remains. That was the axis around which rational people moved, to obtain a point of agreement and a criterion of harmony. So some of them normalized the principle of numbers onto the idea to reach that foundation, and formulated laws and measures based on what ought to be, which was called logic.
It began with the beginning of organized human thought when the Greek philosopher Aristotle (384-322 BC) refined its issues and chapters, and placed the first collection of his files, known as (the Organon), which included the following books: (Categories - On Interpretation - Prior Analytics - Posterior Analytics - Topics - Sophistics), and to them are added (Rhetoric - Poetics). Aristotle's books on logic were translated into Arabic in the second century AH, and it is said in the first century by the Syrian translators, the most famous of whom was Ishaq ibn Hunayn (d. 950 CE), who translated the Categories.
Then came the attempt of Porphyry, from the people of the coastal city of Tyre in southern Lebanon (233-304 BC), when he wrote his book (Isagoge), which is a Greek word meaning (Introduction), which is the second name of this book because it discusses the five universals. It was translated into Arabic by Abu Uthman al-Dimashqi in the ninth century AD, and abridged by Athir al-Din al-Mufaddal ibn Umar al-Abhari (d. 663 AH / 1264 CE). Many commentaries and glosses were written on it. Then Galen (d. 160 CE) added the fourth figure to the three figures. Among the commentaries written on it, the most important was the Book of Doubts by Abu Zakariyya al-Razi. Then a great development occurred in its subjects, after the eight heads were available, so it became an independent science. If you say: Since the subjects of logic are rational measures, why the disagreement? We say: The answer to that can be at two levels. The first level: Differences in visions in explaining those rules do not imply contradiction; rather, multiple attempts occur to convey their applications to minds, which is known as commentaries and glosses on those rules. The second level: The human intellect is in continuous developmental movement, so we need new rules that accord with its contemporary stage.
What we are undertaking is one of those attempts, which came in the fifth century AH, written by the philosopher-theologian Sayf al-Din al-Amidi (551-631 AH) under the title 'Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq'. It opens with praise of Allah, blessings on the Prophet and his family, then discusses the nature of intellectual disagreement and the origin of logic. D) Ibn Sina

Key Takeaway
Logic evolved through Greek contributions (Porphyry's five universals, Galen's fourth figure) and was critically expanded by Islamic philosophers into an independent science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a chain of transmission for the concept of the 'fourth figure' from Galen through its integration into Islamic syllogistic, naming at least one Islamic scholar who used it.

The Purpose and Structure of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq
Author presents his book Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq and defends the need for new logical works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Amidi wrote Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq and describe its planned scope as presented in the preface.

Core Idea
Al-Amidi's Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq was meant to cover logic, physics, and theology, but only the logical part survives.

The author concludes the preface by introducing his own work: Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq by Sayf al-Din al-Amidi. He explains that it was written in response to a request and was intended to cover three sciences: logic, physics, and theology. However, only the logical section has been found. This lesson focuses on that introduction and the author's response to potential objections about disagreements in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author and his work
Find the name Sayf al-Din al-Amidi and the title 'Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq'. Note the time period (551-631 AH / 12th-13th century CE).
2
Determine the work's intended scope
The author states that the request was to compile a book covering 'the three sciences: logic, physics, and theology (al-mantiqiyya, al-tabi'iyya, al-ilahiyya).'
3
Note the actual extant portion
The author says he only found the logical investigations (al-mabahith al-mantiqiyya).
4
Analyze the defense against disagreement
The author addresses an objection: if logic is rational measures, why disagreement? His two-level answer: (1) differences in explanation do not contradict the rules; (2) human intellect evolves, needing new rules.

الحمد لله الذي تعالى في شهودِه ففاقَ نواظر الأبصار، والصلاةُ والسَّلام على شاهد مشاهد حقيقته مُحمد النّبي في الجهر والإسرار، وعلى أهل بيته شواهد الأقدار، خُزّان علم العزيز الجبّار.
قد يتأرجح العقل في عمليات تصدير المفاهيم للأفعال المترتبة على ما يكمن في حيثياتها، فيختلف ويتخلف في المواءمة حتى في الفكرة الواحدة نفسها، فَنُحَصل على ما يتباين منها، لكن يبقى الاستفهام حول انعدام الاتساق. كان ذلك المحور الذي سار في أفقه المتعقلون، للحصول على نقطة الاتفاق، ومعيار التوافق، فقام بعضهم بتطبيع مبدأ الأعداد على الفكرة للوصول إلى ذلك المرتكز، وصياغة قوانين وموازين على أساس ما ينبغي سُمّي بالمنطق.
بدأ مع بداية التفكير الإنساني المنظم حينما قام الفيلسوف الإغريقي أرسطو طاليس (384 - 322ق.م) بتهذيب مسائله وفصوله، ووضع المجموعة الأولى لملفاته، والتي عُرفت بـ (الأورغان أورغانون ضمت الكتب التالية: (كتاب المقولات – كتاب العبارة - كتاب التحليلات الأولى - كتاب التحليلات الثانية - كتاب الجدل - كتاب الفلسفة)، ويضاف إليها (كتاب الخطابة - كتاب الشعر) وترجمت كتب أرسطو في المنطق إلى العربية في القرن الثاني الهجري، وقيل في القرن الأول من قبل النقلة السريان وأشهرهم إسحاق بن حنين ت) (950م) وهو الذي ترجم كتاب المقولات.
ثم جاءت محاولة فرفريوس من أهالي مدينة صور الساحلية الواقعة في جنوب لبنان 233 - 304ق.م) حينما وضع كتابه (إيساغوجي) وهي كلمة يونانية تعني (المدخل) وهو الاسم الثاني لهذا الكتاب؛ لأنه يبحث في الكليات الخمس، ونقله إلى العربية أبو عثمان الدمشقي في القرن التاسع الميلادي، واختصره أثير الدين المفضل بن عمر الأبهري (ت 663 هـ - (1264م) وكتبت عليه العديد من الشروح - والحواشي المختلفة، المختلفة، ثمّ جالينوس (ت سنة (160م الذي أضاف الشكل الرابع إلى الأشكال الثلاثة، وما كتب عليه من شروح، كان أهمها كتاب الشكوك لأبي زكريا الرازي، وكذا الحال عند فلاسفة الإسلام، حيث وضع العديد منهم والمؤلفات القيمة، كأبي نصر الفارابي (ت 950م) المُلقب بـ (المعلم الثاني)، والشيخ الرئيس ابن سينا، والقطب الرازي والخواجة نصير الدين الطوسي، والقاضي أبي الثناء الأرموي، والعلامة الأخضري، والعلامة الحلي، والسيد الشريف الجرجاني، وكثيرين غيرهم.
فحدث تطور كبير في موضوعاته، بعد أن توافرت الرؤوس الثمانية، فكان علماً مستقلاً بذاته. إن قلت: إن كانت موضوعات علم المنطق موازين عقليّة، فلِمَ الاختلاف؟ قلنا: يمكن جواب ذلك بمستويين: المستوى الأول: اختلاف الرؤى في بيان وشرح تلك القواعد، لا يعني تناقضها، بل تجري المحاولات المتعدّدة لإيصال تطبيقاتها إلى الأذهان، وهو ما يُعرف بالشروح والحواشي على تلك القواعد. المستوى الثاني: العقل البشري في حركة تطويرية مستمرة، فنحتاج إلى قواعد جديدة تتوافق والمرحلة المعاصرة له.
وما نقرّض له هو إحدى تلك المحاولات، جاءت في القرن الخامس الهجري، وضعها الفيلسوف المتكلم سيف الدين الآمدي (551 - 631هـ) تحت عنوان "دقائق الحقائق"، قال في مقدمتها فسألني من تعيّن إجابته، وتحتمت علي تلبيته، جمع کتاب جامع لدقائق الحقائق من العلوم الثلاثة وهي المنطقية، والطبيعية والإلهيّة، لكني لم أعثر إلا على ما يحتوي المباحث المنطقية، تناول فيها مباحث
Praise be to Allah who is exalted in His witnessing, surpassing the sights of eyes. Blessings and peace be upon the witness of the witnessed of His reality, Muhammad the Prophet in public and secret, and upon his family, witnesses of destinies, treasurers of the knowledge of the Mighty, the Compeller.
The intellect sometimes oscillates in the operations of exporting concepts for actions resulting from what lies in their circumstances, so it differs and fails in harmony even in the same idea. We obtain what differs from it, but the question about the lack of consistency remains. That was the axis around which rational people moved, to obtain a point of agreement and a criterion of harmony. So some of them normalized the principle of numbers onto the idea to reach that foundation, and formulated laws and measures based on what ought to be, which was called logic.
It began with the beginning of organized human thought when the Greek philosopher Aristotle (384-322 BC) refined its issues and chapters, and placed the first collection of his files, known as (the Organon), which included the following books: (Categories - On Interpretation - Prior Analytics - Posterior Analytics - Topics - Sophistics), and to them are added (Rhetoric - Poetics). Aristotle's books on logic were translated into Arabic in the second century AH, and it is said in the first century by the Syrian translators, the most famous of whom was Ishaq ibn Hunayn (d. 950 CE), who translated the Categories.
Then came the attempt of Porphyry, from the people of the coastal city of Tyre in southern Lebanon (233-304 BC), when he wrote his book (Isagoge), which is a Greek word meaning (Introduction), which is the second name of this book because it discusses the five universals. It was translated into Arabic by Abu Uthman al-Dimashqi in the ninth century AD, and abridged by Athir al-Din al-Mufaddal ibn Umar al-Abhari (d. 663 AH / 1264 CE). Many commentaries and glosses were written on it. Then Galen (d. 160 CE) added the fourth figure to the three figures. Among the commentaries written on it, the most important was the Book of Doubts by Abu Zakariyya al-Razi. Likewise the case among the philosophers of Islam, where many of them wrote valuable works, such as Abu Nasr al-Farabi (d. 950 CE) nicknamed 'the Second Teacher', and the Chief Master Ibn Sina, and Qutb al-Din al-Razi, and Khwaja Nasir al-Din al-Tusi, and Qadi Abi al-Thana al-Urmawi, and al-Allama al-Akhdari, and al-Allama al-Hilli, and al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani, and many others.
Then a great development occurred in its subjects, after the eight heads were available, so it became an independent science. If you say: Since the subjects of logic are rational measures, why the disagreement? We say: The answer to that can be at two levels. The first level: Differences in visions in explaining those rules do not imply contradiction; rather, multiple attempts occur to convey their applications to minds, which is known as commentaries and glosses on those rules. The second level: The human intellect is in continuous developmental movement, so we need new rules that accord with its contemporary stage.
What we are undertaking is one of those attempts, which came in the fifth century AH, written by the philosopher-theologian Sayf al-Din al-Amidi (551-631 AH) under the title 'Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq'. He said in its preface: 'One whom it was incumbent to answer asked me, and whose request was obligatory to fulfill, to compile a comprehensive book of the subtleties of truths from the three sciences, namely logic, physics, and theology, but I have only found that which contains the logical investigations.' He discussed in it investigations...
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is the preface (muqaddimah) of the book 'Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq'. It opens with praise of Allah, blessings on the Prophet and his family, then discusses the nature of intellectual disagreement and the origin of logic. The author explains that logic emerged as a tool to standardize thought and achieve consistency. He then provides a historical overview: Aristotle systematized logic with the Organon, translated into Arabic by Syriac scholars like Ishaq ibn Hunayn. Porphyry wrote the Isagoge (introduction) focusing on the five universals; al-Abhari later abridged it. Galen added the fourth figure of the syllogism. Islamic philosophers like al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and others further developed the field. The author notes that logic became an independent science after the 'eight heads' (the eight books of the Organon plus Rhetoric and Poetics). He then addresses a potential objection: if logic is rational, why is there disagreement? He answers on two levels: first, differences in explanation do not mean contradiction; second, human reason evolves, requiring new rules. Finally, he introduces his own work as a contribution in the fifth century AH by al-Amidi, but notes that only the logical portion of the planned three-part work (logic, physics, theology) has been found.

سيف الدين الآمدي
العلوم الثلاثة

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq was conceived as a three-part work covering logic, physics, and theology, but only the logical section is extant, and it was written to address both the need for clarity and the evolving nature of human reason.

The Scope of Logic: Concepts, Assents, and Demonstration
Author outlines the book's subject matter and structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three core subjects of classical logic (tasawwurat, tasdiqat, burhan) and explain the author's methodological approach.

Core Idea
Logic is divided into concepts, assents, and demonstration — the three pillars of certain knowledge.

This lesson introduces the foundational structure of the book. The author states that his work covers concepts (tasawwurat), assents (tasdiqat), and demonstration (burhan). These three categories are the backbone of Islamic logic. Understanding this tripartite division is essential before diving into the details of definitions and syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three subjects
Find the three terms that name the main topics of the book: tasawwurat, tasdiqat, burhan. Note that they are the most fundamental divisions in logic.
2
Analyze the methodology
The author uses three verbs: bahth (research), tadqiq (scrutiny), tahqiq (verification). He also mentions discussing scholars' opinions. This sets the tone for a rigorous academic work.
These terms are common in Islamic scholarly writing.
3
Map the book structure
The book is divided into 'arts' (funun), then each art into 'articles' (maqalat), then each article into 'chapters' (fusul). This hierarchical organization is typical in classical texts.
4
Understand the purpose
The author aims to present and verify the opinions of scholars on various issues. The logical framework (concepts, assents, demonstration) provides the tools for this verification.

The methodology involves research, scrutiny, and verification of scholars' opinions, with discussion of their statements on diverse issues. The book is organized into arts (funun) and articles (maqalat), then further divided into chapters (fusul). This sets the stage for a comprehensive logical work.

Editor's Methodology in Critical Editions of Islamic Manuscripts
Editor describes manuscript and explains critical edition method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the main steps involved in editing an Islamic manuscript and explain the purpose of each step.

Core Idea
Every bracket and diacritical mark in a critical edition is a scholarly intervention that brings the reader closer to the author's original text.

The editor’s introduction to a critical edition reveals the painstaking work behind presenting a classical text to modern readers. In this passage, the editor of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq describes his methodology, from the physical description of the manuscript to the conventions he uses. Understanding this process is essential for anyone who relies on critical editions for scholarly work in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the manuscript's provenance
Note where the manuscript is held (Princeton University) and its physical condition. The editor describes moisture damage but notes the original text is unaffected.
The manuscript was read to the author during his lifetime, adding authority.
2
Examine codicological details
Look at the ink, script, number of pages, binding, and line count. These details help identify the manuscript's age and scribal practices.
Black ink fading to brown, small script, 23 lines per page, brown binding.
3
Understand editorial conventions
The editor explains his use of square brackets for additions and his steps of textual correction (dabt al-nass) and punctuation (tanqit).
Everything between [ ] is from the editor, not the original.
4
Recognize the human element
The acknowledgments show the collaborative nature of producing a critical edition. The editor thanks scholars and urges attention to Islamic heritage.

المنهج المتبع في التحقيق 1- هذه المخطوطة موجودة في خزائن جامعة برنستون الأميركية، وهي من المخطوطات الجميلة، التي ما زالت تحافظ على رونقها، تظهر عليها بعض عوامل الزمن من الرطوبة، إلا أنها لم تؤثر على أصل المخطوطة، والنسخة غير مؤرخة، لكن الشيء اللافت للنظر أن المخطوطة عُرضت على المؤلف، وقُرئت عليه في حياته من قبل الخطاط، كما تشير بعض الصفحات، كما في الصفحة 195ب، و 198 أ ، عدد صفحات الكتاب 495 صفحة، هناك سبع ورقات فارغة في بداية المخطوطة وتسع ورقات فارغة في نهايتها، وكانت قليلة السقط، كتبت بالمداد الأسود الذي يتلاشى إلى البني، ذات خط صغير نسبياً، وكانت نهاية بعض الأسطر منها يكمله على السطر بعد فراغ في الحاشية، تحتوي كل صفحة على 23 سطراً، أما الجلد فهو باللون البني. -2 بعد كتابة المخطوطة، تم ضبط النص وتصحيحه. -3 إضافة علامات التنقيط. -4 كل ما بين [] فهو إضافة من المحقق. وفي ختام هذا التقريض، أقدم شكري وامتناني إلى الأخ الشيخ عمار التميمي والشيخ إبراهيم فائق البصري على كل ما بذلاه لإخراج هذا الكتاب إلى النور، كما أشكر كل من ساهم في ذلك، وأدعو الباحثين والمحققين، للالتفات إلى تراث الأمة الإسلامية القابع على رفوف المكتبات في شتى فنون المعرفة الإنسانية، ملتمساً من المولى سبحانه تعالى أن يوفقنا لما فيه الخير والصلاح والحمد لله أولاً وآخراً. فاضل علي الموسوي النجف الأشرف الأول من محرم الحرام 1440هـ
The methodology followed in the investigation: 1- This manuscript is located in the libraries of Princeton University in America, and it is among the beautiful manuscripts that still retain their luster. Some signs of age from moisture appear on it, but they have not affected the original manuscript. The copy is undated, but the striking thing is that the manuscript was presented to the author and read to him during his lifetime by the calligrapher, as indicated by some pages, such as page 195b and 198a. The number of pages of the book is 495 pages. There are seven blank folios at the beginning of the manuscript and nine blank folios at its end. It had few lacunae. It was written in black ink that fades to brown, with relatively small script. The end of some lines is completed on the line after a space in the margin. Each page contains 23 lines. The binding is brown. 2- After writing the manuscript, the text was checked and corrected. 3- Adding punctuation marks. 4- Everything between [ ] is an addition by the editor. At the conclusion of this foreword, I extend my thanks and gratitude to brother Sheikh Amar al-Tamimi and Sheikh Ibrahim Fayiq al-Basri for all they did to bring this book to light, and I thank everyone who contributed. I call upon researchers and editors to pay attention to the heritage of the Islamic nation lying on library shelves in various fields of human knowledge, beseeching the Almighty to grant us success in what is good and righteous. Praise be to God first and last. Fadil Ali al-Mousawi, Najaf al-Ashraf, 1st of Muharram 1440 AH.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is the editor's description of the manuscript and his editorial methodology. The editor first describes the physical manuscript: its location at Princeton, its good condition despite moisture, its undated nature, and the remarkable fact that it was read to the author during his lifetime. He provides codicological details: number of pages, blank folios, script, ink, line count, and binding. Then he outlines his editorial steps: collation and correction, addition of punctuation, and marking editorial additions with brackets. Finally, he acknowledges those who helped and urges scholars to engage with Islamic heritage. This passage is typical of a critical edition's introduction.

Assessing Manuscript Reliability in Mantiq
Describes the manuscript source and the editor's approach to verification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess the reliability of a critical edition of an Islamic logical text by examining its manuscript source and editorial methodology.

Core Idea
A true mantiq scholar works from verified manuscripts, not assumptions.

Before diving into the logical arguments of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, we must ensure the text we have is trustworthy. This lesson walks you through the editor's methodology for this edition, so you can critically evaluate the source material for any logical work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the manuscript's provenance
Locate where the manuscript is held and note any evidence of age and chain of custody. The editor tells us it is at Princeton University and that it was presented to the author during his lifetime.
2
Examine indications of authorial supervision
The most striking fact is that the manuscript was read back to the author (al-Amidi) by the calligrapher, as indicated on pages 195b and 198a. This greatly enhances its authority.
3
Assess physical completeness and condition
Check for blank pages, lacunae, and signs of age. The manuscript has 495 pages, 7 blank folios at the start, 9 at the end, few missing parts, and some moisture damage that did not affect the text.
4
Understand editorial interventions
The editor collated and corrected the text, added diacritical marks, and placed editorial additions in brackets []. Transparency in editing allows you to distinguish the original from the editor's work.
5
Evaluate overall trustworthiness
Weigh all factors: authorial review, physical state, and editorial care. This manuscript is reliable because it was read to the author and handled with methodological rigour.

-2 بعد كتابة المخطوطة، تم ضبط النص وتصحيحه. -3 إضافة علامات التنقيط.
-4 كل ما بين [] فهو إضافة من المحقق.
وفي ختام هذا التقريض، أقدم شكري وامتناني إلى الأخ الشيخ عمار التميمي والشيخ إبراهيم فائق البصري على كل ما بذلاه لإخراج هذا الكتاب إلى النور، كما أشكر كل من ساهم في ذلك، وأدعو الباحثين والمحققين، للالتفات إلى تراث الأمة الإسلامية القابع على رفوف المكتبات في شتى فنون المعرفة الإنسانية، ملتمساً من المولى سبحانه تعالى أن يوفقنا لما فيه الخير والصلاح والحمد لله أولاً وآخراً.
The methodology followed in verification: 1- This manuscript is located in the holdings of Princeton University in America, and it is one of the beautiful manuscripts that still retain their luster. Some effects of time from moisture appear on it, but they did not affect the original manuscript. The copy is undated, but the striking thing is that the manuscript was presented to the author, and it was read to him during his lifetime by the calligrapher, as some pages indicate, as on page 195b and 198a. The number of pages of the book is 495 pages. There are seven blank folios at the beginning of the manuscript and nine blank folios at its end. It had few lacunae. It was written in black ink fading to brown, with relatively small script. The end of some lines was completed on the line after a space in the margin. Each page contains 23 lines. As for the binding, it is brown. 2- After writing the manuscript, the text was collated and corrected. 3- Addition of diacritical marks. 4- Everything between [ ] is an addition by the editor. At the conclusion of this endorsement, I extend my thanks and gratitude to brother Shaykh Ammar al-Tamimi and Shaykh Ibrahim Fayiq al-Basri for all their efforts in bringing this book to light, and I thank everyone who contributed to that. I urge researchers and editors to pay attention to the heritage of the Islamic nation sitting on library shelves in various fields of human knowledge, beseeching the Exalted Lord to guide us to what is good and righteous. Praise be to God first and last.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction describing the methodology followed in preparing the critical edition of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq. The passage opens by specifying that the manuscript used is from Princeton University. The editor notes that while the manuscript shows signs of age, it is in good condition. The most remarkable fact is that the manuscript was read back to the author (Seif al-Din al-Amidi) during his lifetime, making it a highly authoritative copy. The editor provides details: the book has 495 pages, with blank folios at the beginning and end, and few missing sections. The script is small, black ink fading to brown, with some lines completed in the margin. Each page has 23 lines, and the binding is brown. Then the editor lists the steps taken: collation and correction of the text, addition of diacritical marks, and marking any editorial additions with brackets. Finally, the editor thanks those who helped and encourages scholars to attend to the Islamic intellectual heritage. This passage sets the context for the reliability of the edition, which is important for students of mantiq who rely on the accuracy of the text.

Al-Amidi's Lament: Separation of Instrumental and Transmitted Sciences
Author laments neglect of rational sciences and defends logic as essential for religious sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between instrumental sciences (ulūm al-ālah) and transmitted sciences (al-manqūl) as al-Amidi uses them, and explain why he laments their separation.

Core Idea
Instrumental sciences like logic are tools that safeguard transmitted sciences, not enemies of them.

Al-Amidi opens his work with a lament. Many in his time have turned away from the instrumental sciences, treating them as separate from the transmitted sciences of revelation. But for al-Amidi, this separation is a disaster: it leads to neglect of both learning and religious duty. Let's parse his complaint.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of sciences
Search the Arabic for 'علوم الآلة' and 'المنقول'. What do these terms refer to? Instrumental sciences are tools (like logic and grammar); transmitted sciences are based on revelation (like hadith and tafsir).
These terms are used throughout Islamic intellectual history.
2
Find the author's description of the people's actions
Look for the statement that people have intensified diverting instrumental sciences from transmitted sciences. The translation suggests 'اشتد أكثر سعفان زماننا وعلماواتا بالتطريع علوم الآلة لبن المنقول'. What does this tell you about their attitude?
The OCR is corrupt; we rely on the translation.
3
Understand the consequences he implies
Why is this diversion harmful? How does it affect religious matters? Al-Amidi suggests it leads to contempt for religious obligations and hypocrisy.
4
Note the author's own stance
Is al-Amidi for or against instrumental sciences? How do you know? He clearly values them as tools necessary for proper religious understanding.

In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Your help is the center. The Sayyid, the Imam, the scholar, the virtuous, the leader of the community, Muhammad al-Islam, Abu al-Husayn Ali ibn Muhammad al-Amidi said: He praised God for His blessings and the gifts of His giving, praise... Thereafter: The majority of the palmy [؟] of our time and our scholars [؟] have intensified in diverting the instrumental sciences from the transmitted [؟] ... they protect the expenditure of that when the legal matters and religious issues [؟] and some of them carry that state upon the [؟] theories of contempt by abandoning the commanded and committing the forbidden, pretending that it is [؟] of the negligent and the virtue of the pretenders, while the most beautiful of people in knowledge [؟] and the farthest of them from sufficient knowledge [؟] with what they hear of noble expressions and foreign names such as Aniyuli [؟] and the element and the matter and the form and the first cause and the active intellect from Socrates, Hippocrates, Plato, Aristotle, Themistius, [؟], Proclus, Alexander of Aphrodisias, and the like. The utmost of the pretenders among them is knowledge of the linguistic appearances without the hidden inner secrets. And if they had looked into the reality of the sciences of the ancients and their depths or preserved the observation of their secrets, they would have found the noble ones [؟] hypocrites of religion and not contrary to the methodologies of the Muslims. And that is like the logical sciences that lead to the investigation of the means that lead to the knowledge of noble things [؟] and the illuminating statements, and like the mathematical sciences of arithmetic and geometry, and most of the issues of the natural and divine sciences [؟]
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a preface or front matter from the book Daqaiq Haqaiq by al-Amidi. The author begins with the basmala and praises God. He then laments the state of his time, noting that many people have diverted the instrumental sciences (like logic) away from the transmitted sciences. Some people even engage in contempt for religious obligations, pretending to be virtuous while they are actually negligent. Al-Amidi criticizes those who are impressed by foreign terms and names from Greek philosophers (Socrates, Hippocrates, Plato, Aristotle, etc.) but only grasp superficial linguistic meanings without understanding the deeper truths. He argues that if these people truly knew the ancient sciences, they would find them compatible with religion, not contradictory. He specifically mentions logic and mathematics as examples of sciences that are in harmony with Islamic methodology.

Al-Amidi laments that people in his time have divorced instrumental sciences from transmitted ones, treating them as separate, which leads to neglect of both reason and religion.

Al-Amidi's Defense: Ancient Sciences Are Compatible with Islam
Author laments neglect of rational sciences and defends logic as essential for religious sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Amidi's argument that true knowledge of ancient sciences (logic, mathematics) is not contrary to Islam but supports it.

Core Idea
When properly understood, Greek logic and mathematics are in harmony with Islamic methodology.

Al-Amidi not only laments but also defends. He argues that those who are impressed by Greek names but only grasp surface meanings miss the deeper compatibility. If they truly studied the ancient sciences, they would see that they are not hypocritical of religion. Let's unpack his defense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'foreign names' mentioned
Look for the list of Greek philosophers: Socrates, Hippocrates, Plato, Aristotle, etc. Why does al-Amidi mention them? He is criticizing those who admire these names without understanding the substance.
2
Find the critique of superficial learning
Al-Amidi says the utmost of these pretenders is 'knowledge of the linguistic appearances without the hidden inner secrets.' What does this mean? They only care about the surface of Greek terms, not their true meaning.
3
Locate the conditional claim
Find the sentence: 'If they had looked into the reality of the sciences of the ancients... they would have found them not contrary to Islam.' This is his key defense.
4
Note the examples of compatible sciences
Al-Amidi specifically mentions logical sciences, mathematical sciences (arithmetic and geometry), and most issues of natural and divine sciences. These are examples of ancient sciences that are compatible.
5
Summarize his conclusion
Al-Amidi concludes that these sciences lead to knowledge of noble things and are in harmony with Islamic methodologies. They are not hypocritical of religion when properly understood.

Al-Amidi's Critique of Superficial Engagement with Greek Philosophy
Al-Amidi opens the work with praise, states his motivation, and warns against superficial knowledge of Greek philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify al-Amidi's critique of superficial engagement with Greek philosophy and distinguish it from a wholesale rejection of philosophy.

Core Idea
Al-Amidi condemns not Greek philosophy itself, but the neglect of religion and pretentious ignorance.

Al-Amidi opens his work with a sharp warning: many scholars of his time have become enamored with Greek philosophy to the point of abandoning religious duties, yet they only parrot impressive terms without understanding. He is not rejecting philosophy outright; rather, he is criticizing those who use it as an excuse for laxity and who mistake jargon for knowledge. This passage sets the stage for his project of distinguishing beneficial from harmful aspects of foreign sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the occasion and motivation
Read the opening lines. Al-Amidi praises God and then states his reason for writing: the excessive fascination of contemporaries with 'sciences of the ancients' has turned them away from religious matters. What is his stated problem?
The phrase 'صدهم عن النظر في الأمور الشرعية' is key: it turned them away from religious considerations.
2
Extract the criticism
Find where al-Amidi describes the behavior of these scholars. They pretend to be wise but are actually ignorant. They abandon commandments and commit prohibitions, pretending to be 'established philosophers'. What is the core of his accusation?
The criticism is twofold: outward negligence of religion and inner ignorance masked by jargon.
3
Identify the terms and names as examples of empty jargon
List the Greek terms al-Amidi mentions: الهيولى (hyle), العنصر (element), الأسطقس (stoicheion), المادة (matter), الصورة (form), العلة الأولى (first cause), العقل الفعّال (active intellect). Also note the names of philosophers: Socrates, Hippocrates, Plato, etc. Why does al-Amidi think these are used superficially?
The terms are impressive but the users do not grasp their deeper meanings (المعاني والأسرار الباطنة الخفية).
4
Note al-Amidi's conclusion: not rejection but discernment
Read the final sentence: 'لوجدوا أكثرها غير منافٍ لقواعد الدين ولا مخالف لمناهج المسلمين'. What does al-Amidi claim would happen if they truly understood these sciences? How does this nuance his stance?
He does not condemn the sciences themselves; he condemns the shallow engagement. True understanding would reveal compatibility with Islam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

نحمد الله على نعمائه وإسباغ عطائه حمدا يليق بجلاله ويستحقه بكماله ونسأله الهداية المرضية، والعصمة عن مناهيه والصلاة على محمد سيد الأوّلين والآخرين وعلى آله وأصحابه أعلام الدين.

The noble master, the eminent scholar, the virtuous, Sayf al-Din, the pillar of the religion, the proof of Islam, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Abi Ali ibn Muhammad al-Amidi—may God perpetuate his success—said: We praise God for His favors and the abundance of His gifts, a praise befitting His majesty and worthy of His perfection, and we ask Him for guidance that pleases Him and protection from His prohibitions. And blessings upon Muhammad, the master of the first and the last, and upon his family and companions, the banners of religion. To proceed: For when the passion of the people of our time and the scholars of our era increased for the study of the sciences of the ancients and what is transmitted from the earlier philosophers, to the extent that it turned them away from considering religious matters and divine issues—and perhaps that led some of them to increase in showing disregard by abandoning what is commanded and committing what is prohibited, pretending that they are among the established philosophers and profound scholars, while they are the most ignorant of people concerning what they claim and the farthest from knowledge of what they are involved in, deceived by the grandiose expressions and strange names they hear, such as (hyle), (element), (stoicheion), (matter), (form), (first cause), (active intellect), from Socrates, Hippocrates, Plato, Aristotle, Pythagoras, Proclus, Alexander of Aphrodisias and the like—the utmost knowledge of a profound one among them is only the surface of verbal expressions, not the inner meanings and hidden secrets. By my life, if they were to attain the reality of the sciences of those people and their depths, and encompass their meanings and secrets, they would find most of them not contrary to the principles of religion nor opposed to the ways of Muslims.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction to his work. Al-Amidi begins with the basmala and praise of God, then explains his motivation for writing. He observes that many contemporaries have become excessively fascinated with the sciences of the ancients (Greek philosophy) to the point that they neglect Islamic religious duties. Worse, some pretend to be deep philosophers but are actually ignorant, merely parroting impressive terms like 'hyle', 'element', 'matter', 'form', 'first cause', 'active intellect', and the names of famous philosophers. Their knowledge is only superficial—they know the words but not the true meanings. The author asserts that if they truly understood these sciences, they would find that most of it does not contradict Islamic principles. This sets the stage for the work, which will presumably clarify the relationship between philosophy and religion.

الهيولى
العقل الفعّال

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi criticizes not Greek philosophy itself, but the superficial and negligent engagement with it that leads to abandoning religion; true understanding reveals that most of it is compatible with Islam.

Introduction and Front Matter

Al-Amidi's Critique of Superficial Philosophers
Critique of contemporary scholars' engagement with Greek philosophy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two criticisms al-Amidi makes against his contemporaries who study Greek philosophy and explain his defense of the compatibility of most philosophical sciences with Islam.

Core Idea
Authentic knowledge requires depth, not jargon; most Greek sciences harmonize with Islam.

Al-Amidi begins his book by observing a troubling trend: scholars and students are so enamored with Greek philosophy that they neglect religious duties, often displaying superficial knowledge. He argues that true understanding reveals most of these sciences are not contrary to Islamic principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem al-Amidi describes
Read the opening: 'فإنه لما كثر شغف أبناء زماننا...' What has diverted people from religious matters?
Look for the contrast between outward fascination and inward emptiness.
2
Find the two criticisms
First, they neglect religious obligations while pretending to be philosophers. Second, they only know meaningless terms like 'hyle', 'active intellect', and names of Greek thinkers.
The criticism is both practical (abandoning religion) and intellectual (superficiality).
3
Extract the defense of philosophy
Al-Amidi says: 'لوجدوا أكثرها غير منافٍ لقواعد الدين...' He claims that if they truly understood, they would find most sciences compatible.
He gives examples: logic, mathematics, most natural and divine sciences.
4
Contrast the superficial with the profound
Notice the distinction between 'الظواهر اللفظية' (verbal appearances) and 'المعاني والأسرار الباطنة الخفية' (inner meanings and hidden secrets).
This establishes the need for genuine learning, which the book aims to provide.
