دقائق الحقائق: الجزء الأول / مقدمة المؤلف
كـ (العلوم (المنطقية الراجعة إلى تحقيق الطرق الموصلة إلى معرفة المجهولات كـ (الحجج) و(الأقوال الشارحة، وكـ (العلوم الرياضية من الحسابية، والهندسية، وأكثر مسائل العلوم الطبيعية والإلهية، وأن ما شذ منها خلاف ما رأيت له على محك النظر العلمي والقانون الحكمي، وعلموا أنه لا مستند لهم في إهمال الأمور الشرعية والقضايا الدينية، غير اتباع الأمور الفاسدات والميل إلى مظان الرخص والإباحات، فسألني من تعينت إجابته، وتحتمت علي تلبيته، جمع كتاب جامع لدقائق الحقائق من العلوم الثلاثة وهي المنطقية والطبيعية، والإلهية، كاشف لأغوارها، مبين ،العوارها منبه على دقائقها معرّف لحقائقها، فسارعت لدعوته، مجيباً لإشارته مع كثرة الموانع، وغلبة القواطع إلى وضع هذا الكتاب، وترتيب هذا العجاب وأودعته ما حصلته من غوامض الأسرار، ونتائج الأفكار، في مباحثاتي ومطارحاتي ، وأشرت فيه إلى الكشف عن واقع زلات المتقدمين، والإبانة عن مواضع تحريفات المحرفين في كل موضع أمكن على حسبه، وأتيتُ فيه بالعجب العجاب، ولباب الألباب متجنباً الإسهاب وغَتِ الإطناب، على وجه لا شذَّ عنه شيء من أفكار الأولين، ولا عزب عنه بحث من مباحث المتأخرين، وسلكتُ في ترتيبه قانون الأولين من النظر في العلم المنطقي أولاً، ثم في العلم الطبيعي ثانياً، ثم في العلم الإلهي في ما بعده في العلم الإلهي في ما بعده ثالثاً، وهو المسؤول أن وهو المسؤول أن يعصمنا من الزلل والخلل، وأن يوفقنا للصواب في القول والعمل، إنه أرجى مسؤول وأقرب
مأمول
Your help, O Allah. Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Author's Introduction. The master, the most noble, the imam, the scholar, the virtuous, Sayf al-Din, the pillar of the religion, the proof of Islam, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Abi Ali ibn Muhammad al-Amidi – may Allah prolong his success. We praise Allah for His blessings and the abundance of His gifts, a praise befitting His majesty and worthy of His perfection; and we ask Him for the right guidance and protection from His prohibitions; and blessings upon Muhammad, the master of the first and the last, and upon his family and his companions, the signs of the religion. To proceed: Since the intense passion of the people of our time and the scholars of our era for studying the sciences of the ancients and the transmitted [knowledge] of the ancient philosophers has reached such an extent that it has diverted them from considering religious matters and theological issues, and perhaps this has led some of them to frequently show disdain by abandoning what is commanded and committing what is forbidden, pretending to be among the established philosophers and profound scholars, while they are the most ignorant of people regarding what they claim and the furthest in knowledge of what they are involved in, deceived by what they hear of terrifying phrases and strange names like 'hyle', 'element', 'element', 'matter', 'form', 'first cause', 'active intellect', from Socrates, Hippocrates, Plato, Aristotle, Pythagoras, Proclus, Alexander of Aphrodisias and the like. And the utmost of the profound among them is knowledge of verbal appearances without the meanings and hidden inner secrets. By my life, if they had realized the true nature of those people's sciences and their depths, and encompassed their meanings and secrets, they would find most of them not conflicting with the rules of religion nor contradicting the ways of Muslims. [This is] like the logical sciences which return to verifying the methods leading to knowledge of unknowns, like proofs and explicative statements; and the mathematical sciences, like arithmetic and geometry; and most of the issues of the natural and divine sciences. And that which deviates from that is contrary to what I have seen based on the touchstone of scientific investigation and philosophical law; and they would know that they have no support for neglecting religious matters and theological issues except following corrupt matters and inclining towards places of concessions and permissions. So, someone whose request was incumbent upon me and whose invitation was obligatory asked me to compile a comprehensive book containing the subtleties of truths from the three sciences – namely logic, physics, and metaphysics – revealing their depths, clarifying their flaws, drawing attention to their subtleties, and defining their realities. So I hastened to respond to his call and answer his indication, despite many obstacles and overwhelming hindrances, to write this book and arrange this wonder. I have deposited in it what I have obtained of obscure secrets and results of thoughts from my discussions and debates. I have pointed out in it the uncovering of the actual errors of the predecessors and the clarification of the places of distortions of the distorters in every possible place according to its measure. I have produced in it the wonder of wonders and the quintessence of quintessences, avoiding prolixity and tedium, in a manner such that nothing of the thoughts of the predecessors is omitted, nor any discussion of the later scholars escapes. I have followed in its arrangement the method of the predecessors: considering first the logical science, then the natural science, and then the divine science thereafter as third. And He is the One asked to protect us from slipping and mistake, and to grant us success in speech and action. Indeed, He is the most hoped-for and nearest of hopes.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction (muqaddimat al-mu'allif) to his book 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq'. Al-Amidi begins with the basmala and praise of Allah, followed by a dedication to the Prophet and his companions. He then sets the context: many contemporary scholars and students have become preoccupied with the sciences of the ancients (i.e., Greek philosophy) to the point of neglecting Islamic religious duties. Some even pretend to be profound philosophers while only knowing superficial terms like 'hyle' (hayula), 'element' ('unsur), 'matter' (madda), 'form' (sura), 'first cause', 'active intellect', and names of Greek philosophers. Their knowledge is limited to verbal appearances, not real meanings. Al-Amidi argues that if they truly understood these sciences, they would find most of them not contrary to Islamic rules. He specifically mentions the logical sciences (which deal with proofs and explicative statements), mathematics (arithmetic and geometry), and most natural and divine science issues. What deviates from this is based on corrupt reasoning. Therefore, someone (perhaps a patron) asked him to write a comprehensive book covering logic, physics, and metaphysics, revealing their truths and errors. He compiled it despite many hindrances, including corrections of predecessors' mistakes and later distortions. He followed the order: logic first, then physics, then metaphysics. He concludes with a prayer for divine protection and guidance.

Key Terms
الظواهر اللفظية (al-zawahir al-lafziyya): Verbal appearances; superficial knowledge of terms without understanding their meanings. [Note: No shift.]
الألفاظ الهائلة (al-alfaz al-ha'ila): Terrifying or imposing phrases; jargon that intimidates. [Note: No shift.]
الأسرار الباطنة الخفية (al-asrar al-batina al-khafiyya): Hidden inner secrets; the deeper meanings of a science. [Note: Used in mystical contexts as well; here refers to philosophical depth.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, what would one find if they truly understood the sciences of the ancients?
A) They are completely incompatible with Islam.
B) Most of them do not conflict with religion.
C) They are purely speculative with no benefit.
D) They are only useful for mathematics.

Key Takeaway
True knowledge of philosophy reveals its harmony with Islam; the problem lies in superficial learning and neglect of religious duties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a contemporary claim that 'Islamic science' conflicts with modern science. Using al-Amidi's method, list what is genuine knowledge vs. superficial appropriation.
?
Read a passage from a modern Muslim apologist who reconciles evolution and creation. Apply al-Amidi's test: does the author show depth or superficiality? Does the claim conflict with 'قواعد الدين'?

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Definition of Logic and the Three Sciences
Definition of the logical sciences and enumeration of the three sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain al-Amidi's definition of logic and list the three sciences covered in the book.

Core Idea
Logic is the instrument for discovering unknowns through proof and definition.

In his introduction, al-Amidi provides a concise definition of the logical sciences: they are the methods that lead to knowledge of the unknown, primarily through proofs (hujaj) and explicative statements (aqwal shariha). He then names the three sciences he will cover: logic, physics, and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of logic
Find the phrase: 'العلوم المنطقية الراجعة إلى تحقيق الطرق الموصلة إلى معرفة المجهولات كالحجج والأقوال الشارحة'
Note that logic is presented as a set of methods, not a subject matter about beings.
2
Break down the definition
Identify two components: 'الطرق الموصلة إلى معرفة المجهولات' (methods leading to knowledge of unknowns) and its examples: 'الحجج' (proofs) and 'الأقوال الشارحة' (explicative statements).
These correspond to two main parts of logic: argumentation and definition.
3
Identify the three sciences
Al-Amidi lists: 'العلوم الثلاثة وهي المنطقية والطبيعية، والإلهية'
Ensure you understand that 'الطبيعية' is physics and 'الإلهية' is metaphysics.
4
Relate logic to the other sciences
Logic is the instrument (آلة) for the other sciences; it does not deal with external realities but with the forms of thought.
This reflects the standard Peripatetic view adopted by Ibn Sina and others.

مأمول
Your help, O Allah. Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Author's Introduction. The master, the most noble, the imam, the scholar, the virtuous, Sayf al-Din, the pillar of the religion, the proof of Islam, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Abi Ali ibn Muhammad al-Amidi – may Allah prolong his success. We praise Allah for His blessings and the abundance of His gifts, a praise befitting His majesty and worthy of His perfection; and we ask Him for the right guidance and protection from His prohibitions; and blessings upon Muhammad, the master of the first and the last, and upon his family and his companions, the signs of the religion. To proceed: Since the intense passion of the people of our time and the scholars of our era for studying the sciences of the ancients and the transmitted [knowledge] of the ancient philosophers has reached such an extent that it has diverted them from considering religious matters and theological issues, and perhaps this has led some of them to frequently show disdain by abandoning what is commanded and committing what is forbidden, pretending to be among the established philosophers and profound scholars, while they are the most ignorant of people regarding what they claim and the furthest in knowledge of what they are involved in, deceived by what they hear of terrifying phrases and strange names like 'hyle', 'element', 'element', 'matter', 'form', 'first cause', 'active intellect', from Socrates, Hippocrates, Plato, Aristotle, Pythagoras, Proclus, Alexander of Aphrodisias and the like. And the utmost of the profound among them is knowledge of verbal appearances without the meanings and hidden inner secrets. By my life, if they had realized the true nature of those people's sciences and their depths, and encompassed their meanings and secrets, they would find most of them not conflicting with the rules of religion nor contradicting the ways of Muslims. [This is] like the logical sciences which return to verifying the methods leading to knowledge of unknowns, like proofs and explicative statements; and the mathematical sciences, like arithmetic and geometry; and most of the issues of the natural and divine sciences. And that which deviates from that is contrary to what I have seen based on the touchstone of scientific investigation and philosophical law; and they would know that they have no support for neglecting religious matters and theological issues except following corrupt matters and inclining towards places of concessions and permissions. So, someone whose request was incumbent upon me and whose invitation was obligatory asked me to compile a comprehensive book containing the subtleties of truths from the three sciences – namely logic, physics, and metaphysics – revealing their depths, clarifying their flaws, drawing attention to their subtleties, and defining their realities. So I hastened to respond to his call and answer his indication, despite many obstacles and overwhelming hindrances, to write this book and arrange this wonder. I have deposited in it what I have obtained of obscure secrets and results of thoughts from my discussions and debates. I have pointed out in it the uncovering of the actual errors of the predecessors and the clarification of the places of distortions of the distorters in every possible place according to its measure. I have produced in it the wonder of wonders and the quintessence of quintessences, avoiding prolixity and tedium, in a manner such that nothing of the thoughts of the predecessors is omitted, nor any discussion of the later scholars escapes. I have followed in its arrangement the method of the predecessors: considering first the logical science, then the natural science, and then the divine science thereafter as third. And He is the One asked to protect us from slipping and mistake, and to grant us success in speech and action. Indeed, He is the most hoped-for and nearest of hopes.

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction (muqaddimat al-mu'allif) to his book 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq'. Al-Amidi begins with the basmala and praise of Allah, followed by a dedication to the Prophet and his companions. He then sets the context: many contemporary scholars and students have become preoccupied with the sciences of the ancients (i.e., Greek philosophy) to the point of neglecting Islamic religious duties. Some even pretend to be profound philosophers while only knowing superficial terms like 'hyle' (hayula), 'element' ('unsur), 'matter' (madda), 'form' (sura), 'first cause', 'active intellect', and names of Greek philosophers. Their knowledge is limited to verbal appearances, not real meanings. Al-Amidi argues that if they truly understood these sciences, they would find most of them not contrary to Islamic rules. He specifically mentions the logical sciences (which deal with proofs and explicative statements), mathematics (arithmetic and geometry), and most natural and divine science issues. What deviates from this is based on corrupt reasoning. Therefore, someone (perhaps a patron) asked him to write a comprehensive book covering logic, physics, and metaphysics, revealing their truths and errors. He compiled it despite many hindrances, including corrections of predecessors' mistakes and later distortions. He followed the order: logic first, then physics, then metaphysics. He concludes with a prayer for divine protection and guidance.

Key Terms
المنطقية (al-mantiqiyya): The logical sciences, dealing with methods of proof and definition. [Note: No shift.]
الحجج (al-hujaj): Proofs or arguments leading to certain knowledge. [Note: In jadal, it can refer to dialectical arguments; here it is general.]
الأقوال الشارحة (al-aqwal al-shariha): Explicative statements or definitions that clarify essences. [Note: Equivalent to 'hadd' or 'rasm'.]
الطبيعية (al-tabi'iyya): Natural sciences (physics). [Note: Stable.]
الإلهية (al-ilahiyya): Divine science (metaphysics). [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two types of statements does al-Amidi include as examples under the logical sciences?
A) Proofs and definitions
B) Syllogisms and analogies
C) Inductions and deductions
D) Commands and prohibitions

Key Takeaway
Logic is the science of methods for acquiring knowledge, primarily through proof and definition, and it serves as the foundation for physics and metaphysics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the opening of al-Ghazali's 'al-Mustasfa' where he defines logic similarly. Compare the definitions and note the common elements.
?
Choose a concept from fiqh (e.g., 'salah' or 'zakat') and construct a definition following the pattern of genus and differentia. Then identify whether it is a complete definition (hadd haqiqi) or a description (rasm).

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Method of the Ancients: Order of the Sciences
Author's stated methodology for ordering the sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Amidi's ordering of the sciences (logic, physics, metaphysics) and justify why logic must come first.

Core Idea
Logic precedes the study of nature and divinity because it provides the instrument for correct reasoning.

At the end of his introduction, al-Amidi explains his method: he follows 'the way of the predecessors' (قانون الأولين) by treating logic first, then physics, then metaphysics. This order is not arbitrary; it reflects the logical dependency of these sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the statement of method
Locate: 'وسلكتُ في ترتيبه قانون الأولين من النظر في العلم المنطقي أولاً، ثم في العلم الطبيعي ثانياً، ثم في العلم الإلهي ثالثاً'
He explicitly states he is following the early philosophers (أولين).
2
Understand why logic is first
Logic is the instrument (آلة) that safeguards the mind from error. It must be mastered before studying anything else.
This is a standard view: without logic, one cannot be sure of the correctness of reasoning in other sciences.
3
Physics before metaphysics
According to the Peripatetic tradition, metaphysics is studied after physics because it deals with things beyond nature, and many metaphysical concepts (like the first cause) are argued from physical evidence.
Al-Amidi is not defending this order; he is simply adopting it.
4
Connect to the book's structure
The book 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq' is divided accordingly. This first part (currently studied) covers logic.
Knowing the overall structure helps navigate the text.

كـ (العلوم (المنطقية الراجعة إلى تحقيق الطرق الموصلة إلى معرفة المجهولات كـ (الحجج) و(الأقوال الشارحة، وكـ (العلوم الرياضية من الحسابية، والهندسية، وأكثر مسائل العلوم الطبيعية والإلهية، وأن ما شذ منها خلاف ما رأيت له على محك النظر العلمي والقانون الحكمي، وعلموا أنه لا مستند لهم في إهمال الأمور الشرعية والقضايا الدينية، غير اتباع الأمور الفاسدات والميل إلى مظان الرخص والإباحات، فسألني من تعينت إجابته، وتحتمت علي تلبيته، جمع كتاب جامع لدقائق الحقائق من العلوم الثلاثة وهي المنطقية والطبيعية، والإلهية، كاشف لأغوارها، مبين ،العوارها منبه على دقائقها معرّف لحقائقها، فسارعت لدعوته، مجيباً لإشارته مع كثرة الموانع، وغلبة القواطع إلى وضع هذا الكتاب، وترتيب هذا العجاب وأودعته ما حصلته من غوامض الأسرار، ونتائج الأفكار، في مباحثاتي ومطارحاتي ، وأشرت فيه إلى الكشف عن واقع زلات المتقدمين، والإبانة عن مواضع تحريفات المحرفين في كل موضع أمكن على حسبه، وأتيتُ فيه بالعجب العجاب، ولباب الألباب متجنباً الإسهاب وغَتِ الإطناب، على وجه لا شذَّ عنه شيء من أفكار الأولين، ولا عزب عنه بحث من مباحث المتأخرين، وسلكتُ في ترتيبه قانون الأولين من النظر في العلم المنطقي أولاً، ثم في العلم الطبيعي ثانياً، ثم في العلم الإلهي في ما بعده في العلم الإلهي في ما بعده ثالثاً، وهو المسؤول أن وهو المسؤول أن يعصمنا من الزلل والخلل، وأن يوفقنا للصواب في القول والعمل، إنه أرجى مسؤول وأقرب
مأمول
Your help, O Allah. Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Author's Introduction. The master, the most noble, the imam, the scholar, the virtuous, Sayf al-Din, the pillar of the religion, the proof of Islam, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Abi Ali ibn Muhammad al-Amidi – may Allah prolong his success. We praise Allah for His blessings and the abundance of His gifts, a praise befitting His majesty and worthy of His perfection; and we ask Him for the right guidance and protection from His prohibitions; and blessings upon Muhammad, the master of the first and the last, and upon his family and his companions, the signs of the religion. To proceed: Since the intense passion of the people of our time and the scholars of our era for studying the sciences of the ancients and the transmitted [knowledge] of the ancient philosophers has reached such an extent that it has diverted them from considering religious matters and theological issues, and perhaps this has led some of them to frequently show disdain by abandoning what is commanded and committing what is forbidden, pretending to be among the established philosophers and profound scholars, while they are the most ignorant of people regarding what they claim and the furthest in knowledge of what they are involved in, deceived by what they hear of terrifying phrases and strange names like 'hyle', 'element', 'element', 'matter', 'form', 'first cause', 'active intellect', from Socrates, Hippocrates, Plato, Aristotle, Pythagoras, Proclus, Alexander of Aphrodisias and the like. And the utmost of the profound among them is knowledge of verbal appearances without the meanings and hidden inner secrets. By my life, if they had realized the true nature of those people's sciences and their depths, and encompassed their meanings and secrets, they would find most of them not conflicting with the rules of religion nor contradicting the ways of Muslims. [This is] like the logical sciences which return to verifying the methods leading to knowledge of unknowns, like proofs and explicative statements; and the mathematical sciences, like arithmetic and geometry; and most of the issues of the natural and divine sciences. And that which deviates from that is contrary to what I have seen based on the touchstone of scientific investigation and philosophical law; and they would know that they have no support for neglecting religious matters and theological issues except following corrupt matters and inclining towards places of concessions and permissions. So, someone whose request was incumbent upon me and whose invitation was obligatory asked me to compile a comprehensive book containing the subtleties of truths from the three sciences – namely logic, physics, and metaphysics – revealing their depths, clarifying their flaws, drawing attention to their subtleties, and defining their realities. So I hastened to respond to his call and answer his indication, despite many obstacles and overwhelming hindrances, to write this book and arrange this wonder. I have deposited in it what I have obtained of obscure secrets and results of thoughts from my discussions and debates. I have pointed out in it the uncovering of the actual errors of the predecessors and the clarification of the places of distortions of the distorters in every possible place according to its measure. I have produced in it the wonder of wonders and the quintessence of quintessences, avoiding prolixity and tedium, in a manner such that nothing of the thoughts of the predecessors is omitted, nor any discussion of the later scholars escapes. I have followed in its arrangement the method of the predecessors: considering first the logical science, then the natural science, and then the divine science thereafter as third. And He is the One asked to protect us from slipping and mistake, and to grant us success in speech and action. Indeed, He is the most hoped-for and nearest of hopes.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction (muqaddimat al-mu'allif) to his book 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq'. Al-Amidi begins with the basmala and praise of Allah, followed by a dedication to the Prophet and his companions. He then sets the context: many contemporary scholars and students have become preoccupied with the sciences of the ancients (i.e., Greek philosophy) to the point of neglecting Islamic religious duties. Some even pretend to be profound philosophers while only knowing superficial terms like 'hyle' (hayula), 'element' ('unsur), 'matter' (madda), 'form' (sura), 'first cause', 'active intellect', and names of Greek philosophers. Their knowledge is limited to verbal appearances, not real meanings. Al-Amidi argues that if they truly understood these sciences, they would find most of them not contrary to Islamic rules. He specifically mentions the logical sciences (which deal with proofs and explicative statements), mathematics (arithmetic and geometry), and most natural and divine science issues. What deviates from this is based on corrupt reasoning. Therefore, someone (perhaps a patron) asked him to write a comprehensive book covering logic, physics, and metaphysics, revealing their truths and errors. He compiled it despite many hindrances, including corrections of predecessors' mistakes and later distortions. He followed the order: logic first, then physics, then metaphysics. He concludes with a prayer for divine protection and guidance.

Key Terms
قانون الأولين (qanun al-awwalin): The method or law of the predecessors (the ancient philosophers). [Note: Respectful reference to Greek philosophers, not necessarily in agreement on all points.]
المنطقي (al-mantiqi): The logical science. [Note: No shift.]
الطبيعي (al-tabi'i): The natural science (physics). [Note: No shift.]
الإلهي (al-ilahi): The divine science (metaphysics). [Note: No shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the order in which al-Amidi treats the three sciences?
A) Physics, metaphysics, logic
B) Logic, physics, metaphysics
C) Metaphysics, logic, physics
D) Logic, metaphysics, physics

Key Takeaway
The proper order of study is logic first (as instrument), then physics, then metaphysics, following the established method of the ancients.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the table of contents of a modern Islamic philosophy textbook (e.g., on Islamic theology). Does it follow the same order? If not, why might the author deviate?
?
Write a brief justification (2-3 sentences) for why logic must be studied before physics and metaphysics, using the argument that reasoning tools are needed for any investigation.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

The Three Sciences and Author's Purpose
Author explains motivation, scope, and organization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three sciences covered in Daqaiq al-Haqaiq and explain the author's motivation for writing.

Core Idea
Logic, natural philosophy, and metaphysics form the foundation for understanding religious truths rationally.

The author opens his work by clarifying its scope and purpose. He explains that Islamic scholars must not neglect rational sciences, for they reveal deep truths and correct distortions. Understanding this introduction sets the stage for the rigorous logical analysis that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sciences
Find the explicit enumeration of the three sciences in the introduction. The author mentions them in order: logical (manṭiqī), natural (ṭabīʿī), and divine (ilāhī).
These correspond to the classical trivium of logic, physics, and metaphysics.
2
Understand the author's motivation
Why did he write? He criticizes those who neglect religious sciences under corrupt influences and responds to a request from a person whose call was obligatory. He aims to uncover hidden truths and correct distortions of predecessors.
The author sees his work as a defense of true knowledge against distortion.
3
Note the arrangement
The author follows the 'method of the ancients': logic first, then natural science, then divine science. Think: why start with logic? It provides the tools for reasoning in the other sciences.
This order reflects the Aristotelian curriculum transmitted into Islamic philosophy.

From the logical sciences that return to investigating the ways leading to knowledge of unknowns, such as proofs and explanatory statements, and from the mathematical sciences, from arithmetic and geometry, and most of the questions of the natural and divine sciences; and that what deviates from them contrary to what I have seen upon the touchstone of scientific investigation and legal principle, and they knew that they have no basis in neglecting religious matters and doctrinal issues, except following corrupt matters and inclining to places of concessions and permissions. So one whose answer was incumbent and whose compliance was obligatory asked me to compile a comprehensive book for the deeper truths of the three sciences, which are logical, natural, and divine, uncovering their depths, clarifying their defects, alerting to their subtleties, and defining their realities. So I hastened to his call, responding to his indication despite many obstacles and overwhelming interruptions, to produce this book and arrange this wonder. And I deposited in it what I obtained from obscure secrets and outcomes of thoughts in my discussions and debates. And I pointed in it to uncovering the reality of the slips of the predecessors and clarifying the places of distortions of the distorters in every place possible according to its measure. And I brought in it the wonder of wonders and the core of cores, avoiding prolixity and foul verbosity, in a manner such that nothing of the thoughts of the ancients escapes from it, nor does any discussion of the later scholars stray from it. And I followed in its arrangement the method of the ancients: looking first at the logical science, then at the natural science, then at the divine science after that, thirdly. And He is the one asked to protect us from slip and error, and to grant us success in speech and deed. Indeed, He is the most hopeful of requests and the nearest of hopes. In the first science, which is logic.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is the author's introduction (mukadimah) to his book *Daqaiq al-Haqaiq*. It begins by situating the work within the logical, mathematical, natural, and divine sciences, emphasizing that these disciplines are not opposed to religious matters but rather support them. The author criticizes those who neglect Shar'i sciences under the guise of following corrupt opinions and seeking concessions. He then explains that a request to compile a comprehensive work covering logic, natural philosophy, and metaphysics—the three classical sciences—was made to him. He accepted despite obstacles, aiming to reveal deep truths, expose errors of predecessors, and show where later commentators distorted doctrines. The author promises to be concise yet comprehensive, avoiding superfluous verbosity. Finally, he states that the book follows the ancient arrangement: first logic, then natural science, then divine science. This methodological statement sets the stage for the entire work. The last line, 'In the first science, which is logic,' marks the beginning of the logic section.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Logic as the First Science
Author introduces logic as the first science to be studied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is considered the first science in the Islamic intellectual tradition and identify its role as a foundational instrument.

Core Idea
Logic is the first science because it provides the rules for all correct reasoning.

This opening passage establishes logic as the first science to be studied. The author places it at the beginning of the curriculum, signaling that logic is not merely a subject among others but the instrument (āla) without which no other discipline can be properly acquired. In this lesson, we will examine what it means for logic to be 'first' and how this sets the stage for the entire work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The passage states that the first science is logic. Note the definite article and the phrasing: it asserts a specific hierarchy.
The phrase 'al-'ilm al-awwal' literally means 'the first science.'
2
Understand 'first' in context
In the Islamic curriculum, 'first' can mean first in order of study, or first in rank and importance. Here it likely means both: logic must be studied before other sciences because it provides the method for sound reasoning.
This is analogous to how grammar is studied before other Arabic sciences.
3
Recognize logic as a tool (āla)
The author treats logic as an instrument for all other sciences. This means logic is not studied for its own sake but to equip the student with the rules of correct inference needed in fiqh, kalam, tafsir, etc.
This instrumental view of logic is common in Islamic philosophy.
4
Connect to later critiques
Although this passage asserts logic's primacy, later scholars like Ibn Taymiyya challenged this claim. Recognizing this tension helps appreciate the debates surrounding logic in Islamic thought.
Ibn Taymiyya argued that prophetic knowledge and fitra are superior to Greek logical formalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage opens the book by declaring that the first science to be studied is logic. The author places logic at the beginning of the curriculum, indicating that it is the instrument (āla) for acquiring knowledge in all other disciplines. In the Islamic tradition, logic is considered the foundation because it provides the rules for correct reasoning, without which no other science can be properly understood or defended. This concise statement sets the stage for the detailed exposition of logical concepts that follows.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Classification of Sciences into Practical and Theoretical
Author classifies philosophy into practical and theoretical

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given science as practical or theoretical based on its dependence on human action, and further subdivide theoretical sciences according to the abstraction of their subject from matter.

Core Idea
Sciences are divided by action-dependence and abstraction from matter.

This passage opens a logic work by situating logic as a tool prior to all sciences. The author enumerates the main branches of philosophy: practical and theoretical. Understanding this classification is crucial because it determines the aim and method of each science. We will walk through the criteria used to distinguish these sciences and see how they map to divine, natural, and mathematical sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first division: practical vs theoretical
Notice that the author distinguishes sciences based on whether the subject's existence depends on human action. If it does, it is practical; if not, it is theoretical. Practical sciences aim at knowledge for action, theoretical for knowledge itself.
Practical sciences include ethics and politics; theoretical sciences include metaphysics, physics, and mathematics.
2
Understand the subdivisions of theoretical sciences
The author further divides theoretical sciences based on whether the subject is sought for its own sake (intrinsic) or for something else. Intrinsic sciences are then classified by the degree of abstraction from matter: absolutely abstract, not abstract at all, or abstract in conception only.
This threefold abstraction is the key to mapping the three theoretical sciences.
3
Map each subdivision to a named science
The absolutely abstract (e.g., God, angels) is divine science; the non-abstract (physical bodies) is natural science; the abstract in conception only (numbers, shapes) is mathematical science.
Ensure you understand why mathematical entities are considered abstract in conception but not in external existence.
4
Recognise the ultimate goal of each branch
Practical sciences aim at perfecting worldly order and welfare through action. Theoretical sciences aim at pure knowledge for eternal happiness. This teleological distinction is central to the author's epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علم المنطق
ويشتمل على مقدمتين وتسعة مقالات
المقدمة الأولى: في بيان حصر العلوم، ولزوم تقديم النظر في المنطق منها
نقول: طلب التحصيل إما ليس بحاصل، ومعرفة ما ليس بمعروف، وإما ما وجوده منوط بقدرنا، أو ما لا تعلقه له في جانب وجوده بنا، كما مثل الأول يسمى البحث عنه (فلسفة (عملية كعلم السياسة والأخلاق ونحوهما، وغاية النفس في ذلك معرفة الخير للعمل به والشر لاجتنابه، تتمةً للنظام، وتكملة لصلاح حال الأنام، إلا أن يكون عارفة به فقط، وتفصيل ذلك وتحقيقه في الأكثر موكول إلى الملكوتيين المشرعين من الأنبياء والمرسلين، وما مثل الثاني فيسمى البحث عنه (فلسفة نظرية، وهو إما أن يكون المبحوث عنه مقصوداً في نفسه، أو لا يكون مقصوداً في نفسه بل لغيره، فإن كان مقصوداً في نفسه، فإما أن يكون مجرداً عن المادة مطلقاً، أو أنه لا تجرد له عنها، وإن كان غير مجرد عنها، فإما أن يكون غير مجرد عنها في التصور والوجود الخارجي، أو هو مجرد عنها في التصور لا في الوجود الخارجي، ولا يتصور أن يكون غير مجرد عنها في التصور وهو مجرد عنها في الوجود الخارجي.
فالبحث عن الأول إنما هو في العلم الإلهي، وعن الثاني في العلم الطبيعي
وعن الثالث في العلم الرياضي. وغاية النفس في البحث عن هذه العلوم الثلاثة أن تكون عالمة بالمبحوث عنه فقط، تحصيلاً للسعادة الأبدية بنيل ما لها من الكمال الممكن لها، وإن كان غير
The science of logic. It comprises two introductions and nine articles. The first introduction: on explaining the enumeration of the sciences and the necessity of giving precedence to the study of logic among them. We say: The acquisition of something is either not yet obtained and knowledge of what is not yet known, or its existence is dependent on our power, or it has no connection to us in terms of its existence. As for the first type, the investigation into it is called practical philosophy, such as political science, ethics, and the like. The goal of the soul in that is knowledge of good to act by it and evil to avoid it, as a completion of order and perfection of the welfare of mankind, unless one is merely acquainted with it. The detailed explanation and verification of that is mostly delegated to the heavenly legislators, the prophets and messengers. As for the second type, the investigation into it is called theoretical philosophy. It is either that the thing investigated is intended for its own sake or not intended for its own sake but for something else. If it is intended for its own sake, then it is either abstracted from matter absolutely, or it has no abstraction from it. And if it is not abstracted from it, then it is either not abstracted from it in conception and external existence, or it is abstracted from it in conception but not in external existence. It is inconceivable that it be not abstracted from it in conception while being abstracted from it in external existence. The investigation into the first is in divine science, into the second in natural science, and into the third in mathematical science. The goal of the soul in investigating these three sciences is that it be knowledgeable about the thing investigated only, in order to attain eternal happiness by achieving its possible perfection. And if it is not...
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the introductory section of a logic work. The author first states that the science of logic comprises two introductions and nine articles. He then begins the first introduction, which aims to enumerate the sciences and show the necessity of prioritizing the study of logic. He explains that every inquiry (طلب التحصيل) is either about something not yet obtained (i.e., unknown) and aims to know it, or about something whose existence depends on us (i.e., actions we can perform), or about something that has no connection to us in terms of its existence (e.g., metaphysical matters). The first type is called practical philosophy (فلسفة عملية), which includes political science, ethics, and similar disciplines. Its goal is to know good to act upon it and evil to avoid it, thereby perfecting worldly order and welfare. However, the detailed knowledge of good and evil is mostly entrusted to prophets and messengers. The second type is theoretical philosophy (فلسفة نظرية). Theoretical inquiry is further divided: either the subject is sought for its own sake (i.e., intrinsic knowledge) or for the sake of something else (i.e., instrumental). If it is intrinsic, then the subject may be completely abstracted from matter (like God and angels), or it may not be abstracted at all (like physical bodies), or it may be abstracted in conception but not in external existence (like mathematical entities). The author notes that it is impossible for something to be non-abstract in conception yet abstract in external existence. Based on these distinctions, the three theoretical sciences are: divine science (العلم الإلهي) for what is absolutely abstract, natural science (العلم الطبيعي) for what is not abstract at all, and mathematical science (العلم الرياضي) for what is abstract in conception only. The ultimate aim of these theoretical sciences is pure knowledge for the sake of eternal happiness through the soul's perfection.

Key Terms
فلسفة عملية
فلسفة نظرية
العلم الإلهي
العلم الطبيعي
العلم الرياضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the classification in this passage, which theoretical science studies beings that are abstract from matter in conception but not in external existence?
A) Divine science (al-ilm al-ilāhi)
B) Natural science (al-ilm al-ṭabī'ī)
C) Mathematical science (al-ilm al-riyāḍī)
D) Practical philosophy (al-falsafah al-'amaliyyah)

Key Takeaway
The sciences are primarily divided by whether their subject depends on human action, and theoretical sciences are further classified by the degree of abstraction from matter.

?

Introduction and Front Matter

Classification of Sciences and the Necessity of Logic
Classification of sciences and necessity of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given Islamic science as practical or theoretical and identify whether it is sought for its own sake or as an instrument.

Core Idea
Logic is not another science; it is the tool all sciences depend on.

This opening passage of the book sets the stage for everything that follows. The author presents a sweeping classification of all knowledge, dividing it into practical and theoretical. He then further divides the theoretical into divine, natural, and mathematical sciences based on their relationship to matter. Finally, he isolates logic as a unique instrumental science that must be mastered before any other. Understanding this classification helps you see where each discipline fits and why logic is the first step in the pursuit of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary divisions
Look for the terms 'practical philosophy' (فلسفة عملية) and 'theoretical philosophy' (فلسفة نظرية). Note that the division is based on whether the knowledge is sought for action or for its own sake.
2
Analyze practical sciences
Read the examples given: politics and ethics. Understand that the goal here is to know good and evil in order to act upon them. The author notes that details are mostly left to prophets.
This connects to Islamic law and statecraft.
3
Classify theoretical sciences by abstraction
Within theoretical sciences, the author asks whether the subject is abstract from matter. Three cases: completely abstract (divine science), not abstract at all (natural science), abstract in conception but not in existence (mathematical science).
This is a metaphysical classification based on degrees of immateriality.
4
Locate the instrumental science
The author states that what is not sought for its own sake but for something else is logical science. Logic is the tool that enables the other sciences. The phrase 'الفطرة مما لا تنتقل... دون العلم المنطقي' indicates that natural intellect cannot reach the truths of the other sciences without logic.
5
Determine the priority of logic
Since logic is the instrument for all other sciences, it must be studied first. The conclusion 'فلا جرم أن كان النظر فيه واجباً أولاً' means that investigating logic is the first obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشتمل على مقدمتين وتسعة مقالات المقدمة الأولى: في بيان حصر العلوم، ولزوم تقديم النظر في المنطق منها نقول: طلب التحصيل إما ليس بحاصل، ومعرفة ما ليس بمعروف، وإما ما وجوده منوط بقدرنا، أو ما لا تعلقه له في جانب وجوده بنا، كما مثل الأول يسمى البحث عنه (فلسفة (عملية كعلم السياسة والأخلاق ونحوهما، وغاية النفس في ذلك معرفة الخير للعمل به والشر لاجتنابه، تتمةً للنظام، وتكملة لصلاح حال الأنام، إلا أن يكون عارفة به فقط، وتفصيل ذلك وتحقيقه في الأكثر موكول إلى الملكوتيين المشرعين من الأنبياء والمرسلين، وما مثل الثاني فيسمى البحث عنه (فلسفة نظرية، وهو إما أن يكون المبحوث عنه مقصوداً في نفسه، أو لا يكون مقصوداً في نفسه بل لغيره، فإن كان مقصوداً في نفسه، فإما أن يكون مجرداً عن المادة مطلقاً، أو أنه لا تجرد له عنها، وإن كان غير مجرد عنها، فإما أن يكون غير مجرد عنها في التصور والوجود الخارجي، أو هو مجرد عنها في التصور لا في الوجود الخارجي، ولا يتصور أن يكون غير مجرد عنها في التصور وهو مجرد عنها في الوجود الخارجي. فالبحث عن الأول إنما هو في العلم الإلهي، وعن الثاني في العلم الطبيعي وعن الثالث في العلم الرياضي. وغاية النفس في البحث عن هذه العلوم الثلاثة أن تكون عالمة بالمبحوث عنه فقط، تحصيلاً للسعادة الأبدية بنيل ما لها من الكمال الممكن لها، وإن كان غير مقصود في نفسه فهو العلم المنطقي إذ الفطرة مما لا تنتقل بتحصيل مطلوبات هذه العلوم الثلاثة، ومسائلها في حق الأكثر دون العلم المنطقي كما سنبينه، فلا جرم أن كان النظر فيه واجباً أولاً.
It includes two introductions and nine treatises. The first introduction: on explaining the classification of sciences and the necessity of beginning with logic among them. We say: The pursuit of acquisition is either not yet acquired, and knowledge of what is not known, or it is something whose existence depends on our capacity, or something that has no relation to our existence on its side. As for the first, the inquiry about it is called practical philosophy, like the science of politics, ethics, and the like. The goal of the soul in that is knowledge of good to act upon it and evil to avoid it, as a completion of order and perfection of the rectitude of people's state, unless one knows it only theoretically. The detail and verification of that is mostly delegated to the celestial legislating prophets and messengers. As for the second, the inquiry about it is called theoretical philosophy. It is either that which is sought for its own sake or that which is not sought for its own sake but for something else. If it is sought for its own sake, then either it is abstracted from matter absolutely, or it is not abstracted from it. If not abstracted from it, then either it is not abstracted from matter in conception and external existence, or it is abstracted from matter in conception but not in external existence. It is inconceivable that it be not abstracted in conception but abstracted in external existence. The inquiry about the first is in divine science, about the second in natural science, and about the third in mathematical science. The goal of the soul in investigating these three sciences is only to know the subject matter, attaining eternal happiness by achieving the perfection possible for it. And if it is not sought for its own sake, then it is logical science, for natural disposition does not move to acquire the objects of these three sciences, and their issues are mostly [not] without logical science, as we will clarify. Hence it is necessary that the investigation of it be the first obligation.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is the first introduction of the book, where the author outlines the classification of sciences and argues for the priority of logic. The author begins by stating that the book contains two introductions and nine treatises. The first introduction is on the classification of sciences and the necessity of beginning with logic. He then explains that the pursuit of knowledge is either something not yet attained (practical) or something whose attainment is within our capacity (theoretical). Practical philosophy includes politics and ethics, its aim being to know good for action and evil for avoidance, and its details are mostly left to prophets. Theoretical philosophy is then divided into that sought for its own sake and that sought as an instrument. The sciences sought for their own sake are further divided based on abstraction from matter: divine science (completely abstract), natural science (not abstract in conception or existence), and mathematical science (abstract in conception but not in existence). The goal of these three is knowledge itself for eternal happiness. The science not sought for its own sake but as an instrument is logical science, because the natural intellect cannot attain the objects of the other sciences without it. Therefore, logic must be studied first. The author uses this classification to establish logic as the foundational tool for all other sciences.

External and Intellectual Existence
Author distinguishes two kinds of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external existence (wujud 'ayni) and intellectual existence (wujud 'aqli) and explain why both are considered real.

Core Idea
Both external and intellectual existence are real and invariant across cultures.

In this passage, the author introduces two fundamental kinds of existence: the thing as it is outside the mind, and its representation in the intellect. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the purpose of logic, which is to attain these intellectual representations accurately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of existence
Locate in the Arabic where the author distinguishes existence in itself (external) and existence in the intellect.
The terms are 'وجود عيني' and 'وجود عقلي'.
2
Understand their definitions
External existence is the thing's existence outside the mind; intellectual existence is its mental representation.
The author also calls intellectual existence 'psychic trace' (أثر نفساني).
3
Recognize their reality
The author states both existences are 'real' (حقيقيان) and do not vary with time, nation, or terminology.
This counters the idea that mental existence is merely subjective.
4
See the signifier-signified relationship
Intellectual existence corresponds to external existence and thus can be called a signifier (دال) of the external.
This relationship is crucial for logic and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في بيان وجه الحاجة إلى المنطق، وغايته، وتحقيق موضوع مسائله. نقول: إن كل شيء هو في نفسه ذات وله وجود، وسواء كان محسوساً، أو غير محسوس، كما ستعرفه في ما بعد، فالحاجة ماسة إلى تحصيله في العقل على ما هو عليه مكملاً للنفس الإنسانية، وطلباً للسعادة الأبدية، فهو إن نظر إلى وجوده في ذاته الخارجي عن العقل، فإن كان محسوساً سمي وجوده وجوداً عينياً، ومن أطلق ذلك على غير المحسوس ولم يرد به غير أن له وجوداً في ذاته، فلا مشاحة معه في التسمية، وإن كان بالنظر إلى الوضع متوسعاً، وإن نظر إلى وجوده في العقل قيل له الوجود العقلي والأثر النفساني، وهذان الوجودان حقيقيان – أي لا يختلفان باختلاف الأعصار والأمم - ولا بالنظر إلى الاصطلاحات وأنواع الاختلافات، لكن الوجود العقلي المعبّر عنه بالأثر النفساني من حيث إن شعور النفس بالوجود الخارجي ليس إلا بوساطته، وبسبب مطابقته قد يسمى (دالا)، والوجود الخارجي (مدلولاً)، وكما أن الحاجة داعية إلى التحصيل في العقل لمن ليس بمتحصل، فهي داعية إلى تعريف ما حصل منه في العقل لمن لا تحصل له عنده لحصول المشاركة والاستكمال بالمعاونة، فقد لا يستقل الواحد بنفسه في تحصيل معارفه دون معين ومساعد له من نوعه، فلم يكن بُد من أشياء يُدَلّ بها على ما في الضمير، ولم يكن ذلك أحق من أن يكون فعلاً، ومن الفعل تصويتاً، لا سيما وهو مما لا استقرار له بعدما استغنى عنه بحصول مقصود الإفهام، ولا فيه ضرر الازدحام، فلما كان نوع الإنسان قد خص في تصويته بالعناية الإلهية بتقطيع الحروف، واختلاف وقوعها في حالة تركيبها وإفرادها، كان من مقتضى عناية المبدأ الأول بنوع الإنسان نفسه ما اختلف من رقوم الأصوات المقطعة، وأشكالها المختلفة، أمارات دالة على ما في الضمير من المعاني على اختلافها، إما بطريق التوقيف والوحي منه إلى
The Second Introduction: Exposition of the need for logic, its goal, and verification of the subject of its issues. We say: Every thing in itself is an essence and has existence, whether it is sensible or non-sensible, as you will know later. So the need is pressing to attain it in the intellect as it is, as a perfection for the human soul, and in pursuit of eternal happiness. So if one looks at its existence in its essence external to the intellect, if it is sensible, its existence is called 'external existence'. And whoever applies that to the non-sensible, not meaning by it except that it has existence in itself—there is no quibbling with him over the term, even if from the perspective of usage it is far-fetched. And if one looks at its existence in the intellect, it is called 'intellectual existence' and 'psychic trace'. These two existences are real—that is, they do not vary with variation of ages and nations, nor with respect to terminologies and various differences. However, the intellectual existence, expressed as the psychic trace, insofar as the soul's awareness of external existence is only by means of it, and due to its correspondence it may be called 'signifier', and the external existence 'signified'. And just as the need calls for attainment in the intellect for one who has not attained, so it calls for definition of what has been attained from it in the intellect for one who does not have it in his possession, for the sake of participation and completion through mutual assistance. For an individual may not be independent by himself in acquiring his knowledge without a helper and assistant from his kind. So there was no escape from things by which what is in the mind is indicated, and nothing is more entitled to that than action, and from action sound. Especially since sound has no permanence after the purpose of conveying understanding is achieved, and there is no harm of congestion. So since the human kind was distinguished in his vocalisation by divine care through the articulation of letters and the variation of their occurrence in cases of composition and isolation, it was a consequence of the care of the First Principle for the human kind itself that there varied the marks of articulated sounds and their different forms, as indicative signs of the meanings in the mind according to their diversity, either by way of divine institution and revelation from Him to
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is from the second introduction of Daqaiq Haqaiq, in which the author explains the need for logic, its purpose, and the subject matter of its discussions. The author begins by stating that every thing has an essence and an existence, whether sensible or non-sensible. This is a foundational metaphysical claim. The need for logic arises from the necessity to attain these things in the intellect as they truly are, for the perfection of the human soul and the pursuit of eternal happiness. The author then distinguishes two kinds of existence: external existence ('وجود عيني') which is the thing as it exists outside the mind, and intellectual existence ('وجود عقلي') or psychic trace ('أثر نفساني'), which is the mental representation of the thing. Both are real and do not vary across cultures or terminologies. The intellectual existence is the means by which the soul becomes aware of external existence, and due to its correspondence to the external, it may be called a signifier (دال) and the external existence the signified (مدلول). The author then argues that just as we need to acquire knowledge in our minds, we also need to communicate that knowledge to others for mutual assistance. This necessitates the use of signs to indicate what is in the mind, and the most appropriate signs are actions, specifically vocal sounds. Human speech, with its articulated letters and combinations, is divinely ordained to serve as indicative signs of meanings. These signs may be established by divine revelation (توقيف) or by convention. The passage thus establishes the epistemological and communicative foundations for Logic: the need to accurately represent external realities in the mind and the need to convey those representations through language.

Key Terms
وجود عيني
وجود عقلي
أثر نفساني
دال
مدلول
توقيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The mental representation of a thing
C) A subjective illusion
D) The same as external existence

Key Takeaway
External and intellectual existence are both real and foundational for knowledge.

The Need for Logic: Perfection and Mutual Assistance
Author argues for the necessity of logic and communication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is needed for human perfection and why language must be used to communicate intellectual content.

Core Idea
Logic perfects the soul and enables the mutual assistance required for knowledge.

After establishing the two kinds of existence, the author argues that acquiring intellectual representations is necessary for the soul's perfection and for eternal happiness. Moreover, because humans cannot acquire all knowledge alone, they must communicate through signs, especially speech, which is divinely ordained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of attaining intellectual existence
Find the phrase 'for the perfection of the human soul and pursuit of eternal happiness'. This is the goal of logic.
Logic is not just for argumentation, but for spiritual perfection.
2
Recognize the need for mutual assistance
The author argues that no one can acquire all knowledge alone; we need helpers.
This leads to the need for communication.
3
Understand the role of signs
Signs (things دال on what is in the mind) are necessary for conveying meaning.
Actions and sounds are the most appropriate signs.
4
Appreciate the divine origin of speech
Human speech with articulated letters is a divine gift, according to the author.
This can be either by revelation (توقيف) or convention.

The author then distinguishes two kinds of existence: external existence ('وجود عيني') which is the thing as it exists outside the mind, and intellectual existence ('وجود عقلي') or psychic trace ('أثر نفساني'), which is the mental representation of the thing. Both are real and do not vary across cultures or terminologies. The intellectual existence is the means by which the soul becomes aware of external existence, and due to its correspondence to the external, it may be called a signifier (دال) and the external existence the signified (مدلول). The author then argues that just as we need to acquire knowledge in our minds, we also need to communicate that knowledge to others for mutual assistance. This necessitates the use of signs to indicate what is in the mind, and the most appropriate signs are actions, specifically vocal sounds. Human speech, with its articulated letters and combinations, is divinely ordained to serve as indicative signs of meanings. These signs may be established by divine revelation (توقيف) or by convention. The passage thus establishes the epistemological and communicative foundations for Logic: the need to accurately represent external realities in the mind and the need to convey those representations through language.

The Four Existences and the Need for Logic
Author explains the four degrees of existence and the necessity of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of existence (external, mental, verbal, written) and explain why mental existence is the primary goal of logic.

Core Idea
Logic ensures our mental existence matches external reality, leading to true knowledge and eternal happiness.

The author begins this methodological introduction by explaining why logic is needed. He presents a hierarchy of existence: from external objects to mental concepts, then to spoken words, and finally to written script. Only external and mental existence are real; the others are conventional. Understanding this hierarchy is essential to grasp the purpose of logic: to attain correct mental representations of reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four existences
From the passage, list the four types of existence in order: external (في الأعيان), mental (في العقل), verbal (في اللفظ), and written (في الكتابة). Notice the hierarchy: writing points to speech, speech points to mental impression, mental impression corresponds to external reality.
This hierarchy is key to understanding how knowledge is transmitted.
2
Distinguish real vs. conventional existences
The author states that the first two (external and mental) are real (حقيقيان), while verbal and written existences are not real because they vary by era and community. Highlight the reason: words and writing are based on convention or divine designation, not universal necessity.
This distinction shows why logic focuses on mental content, not language alone.
3
Understand the purpose of mental existence
Find where the author says the greatest purpose among these existences is mental existence (الوجود العقلي). He calls it the perfection of the human soul and the means to eternal happiness. Reflect on why mental representation is so important in Islamic thought.
This connects logic to spiritual perfection.
4
Apply the concept of 'known' vs. 'unknown'
The author concludes: when external existence has a corresponding mental existence, it is called 'known' (معلوم); otherwise it is 'unknown' (مجهول). Logic is the tool to ensure this correspondence. Consider an example: the concept of 'justice' – its external reality (just actions) must be accurately represented in the mind.
This directly shows why logic is necessary for acquiring true knowledge.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دقائق الحقائق: الجزء الأول / المقدمة الثانية: في بيان وجه الحاجة إلى المنطق ببسائط المقاطع اللفظية، على نحو محاذاة المقاطع بها كما سلف، وذلك أيضاً شيء يسير. والأقرب أن ذلك لم يكن إلا أن الغالب في الاستعمال ما كانت الحاجة إليه أمس، وذلك إنما هو دليل الضمير والاستغناء عنه في حق الجميع، وربما وقع الاقتصار عليه في حق الأكثر ، فكان من الواجب أن يكون ذلك ما هو الأخف الأسهل منهما في الإتيان به والتعلم له، وذلك إنما هو اللفظ، فالكتابة دالة على النطق اللساني الدال على الأثر النفساني المطابق للوجود الخارجي، ولذلك قيل إن للشيء وجوداً في الأعيان - أي في الخارج من الأذهان - ووجوداً في العقل، ووجوداً في اللفظ، ووجوداً في الكتابة، لكن الوجودين الأولين حقيقيان كما تقدم، والآخرين فغير حقيقيين؛ لاختلافهما باختلاف الأعصار والأمم، إذ ليس ما وضع من الألفاظ والكتابة الدالة بين قوم بالتوقيف أو الاصطلاح، مما يجب على غيرهم متابعته والمصير إليه، والجمود عليه، بل لو وقع الاصطلاح منهم على غير ذلك لقد كان ذلك في ما يرجع إلى التفاهم على نحو مصطلح الآخرين، كما وقع في الشاهد، وليس أجل المقاصد من هذه الوجودات غير الوجود العقلي وهو الأثر النفساني، إذ هو كمال النفس الإنسانية، وبه حصول السعادة الأبدية كما سبق، فعلى هذا مهما تحقق للوجود الخارجي وجود في العقل مطابق له يسمى إذ ذاك معلوماً، وإلا فمجهول.
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part One / Second Introduction: On explaining the aspect of need for logic with simple verbal components, in the manner of aligning the components as previously mentioned, and that is also a simple matter. The closest [explanation] is that this is only because the majority of usage was in what was most needed, and that is the indication of the conscience and its being dispensed with for everyone, and perhaps it was confined to it for the majority; so it was obligatory that this be the lightest and easiest of the two in terms of bringing it forth and learning it, and that is the word. So writing indicates verbal speech which indicates the psychological impression that corresponds to external existence. Therefore it is said that a thing has existence in external objects (i.e., outside the minds), existence in the mind, existence in speech, and existence in writing. But the first two existences are real, as previously mentioned, while the other two are not real because they differ with different eras and nations; for the words and writing that indicate among a people by divine designation or convention are not something that others must follow, adopt, or adhere to. Indeed, if they had agreed upon something else, that would have been regarding what relates to mutual understanding according to the convention of others, as has occurred in observable reality. And the greatest purpose among these existences is none other than mental existence, which is the psychological impression, for it is the perfection of the human soul, and through it the attainment of eternal happiness, as previously mentioned. So based on this, whenever external existence has a corresponding existence in the mind, it is then called 'known', otherwise it is unknown.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction (المقدمة الثانية) explaining why logic is needed. The author begins by noting that the most common usage in logic is with simple verbal expressions, aligning them as before. He then explains that the reason for this is that what is most needed in usage is the indication of the conscience (ضمير) through words, which is easier than writing. He establishes a hierarchy: writing indicates speech, speech indicates the mental impression, and the mental impression corresponds to external reality. This leads to the famous classification of four existences: external (في الأعيان), mental (في العقل), verbal (في اللفظ), and written (في الكتابة). The first two are real (حقيقيان), while the latter two are conventional and vary across communities. The author emphasizes that the most important existence is mental existence, which is the perfection of the human soul and the key to eternal happiness. When external reality has a mental representation, it is called 'known' (معلوم); otherwise, it is unknown (مجهول). This sets the epistemological foundation for logic, which aims to attain true mental representations.

The Ten Chapters of the First Article on Isaghuji
Author lists the ten chapters of the first article on Porphyry's Isagoge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten chapters of the first article on Isaghuji in order and identify the main topic of each.

Core Idea
The first article on Isaghuji lays out the foundational concepts of logic: from signification to the five universals.

This passage is a roadmap for the first article of a classic logic textbook. It introduces ten chapters that systematically build from basic linguistic concepts to the five universals. Memorizing this structure will help you navigate the entire field of logic as taught in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
First three chapters: Language fundamentals
Chapters 1-3 cover how words signify (lafz), the distinction between simple and compound terms, and the three parts of speech (noun, verb, particle). This is the groundwork for all logical analysis.
Notice the progression from general signification to specific grammatical categories.
2
Chapters 4-5: Meaning and classification
Chapter 4 relates names to their meanings, and Chapter 5 divides simple terms into universal and particular. Both are crucial for understanding definitions and propositions.
The universal-particular division is fundamental for the five universals later.
3
Chapters 6-8: The five universals introduced
Chapter 6 splits the universal into essential and accidental. Chapters 7-8 further divide essential into genus, species, differentia, and accidental into property and general accident. These are the five universals.
This is the core of the Isagoge: the five predicables.
4
Chapters 9-10: Summary and analysis of the five universals
Chapter 9 lists the five universals and surveys previous opinions. Chapter 10 explains their commonalities and differences, providing a concluding synthesis.
These chapters tie together the entire first article.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الأولى في إيساغوجي وتشتمل على عشرة فصول: الفصل الأول: في أقسام دلالات اللفظ. الفصل الثاني: في المفرد والمركب. الفصل الثالث: في تحقيق قسمة المفرد إلى الاسم والكلمة والأداة. الفصل الرابع: في تحقيق نسبة الأسماء إلى معانيها. الفصل الخامس: في تحقيق قسمة المفرد إلى الكلي والجزئي. الفصل السادس: في قسمة الكلي إلى الذاتي والعرضي. الفصل السابع: في قسمة الذاتي إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل. الفصل الثامن: في قسمة العرضي إلى الخاصة والعرض العام. الفصل التاسع: في حصر الكليات الخمس وتتبع ما قيل فيه. الفصل العاشر: في بيان ما تشترك فيه الكليات الخمس، وتفترق وتحقيق ما قيل فيه.
The first article on Isaghuji, and it includes ten chapters: Chapter One: On the divisions of the significations of the word. Chapter Two: On the simple and compound. Chapter Three: On verifying the division of the simple into noun, verb, and particle. Chapter Four: On verifying the relation of names to their meanings. Chapter Five: On verifying the division of the simple into universal and particular. Chapter Six: On the division of the universal into essential and accidental. Chapter Seven: On the division of the essential into genus, species, and differentia. Chapter Eight: On the division of the accidental into property and general accident. Chapter Nine: On enumerating the five universals and examining what has been said about them. Chapter Ten: On explaining what the five universals share and differ in, and verifying what has been said about them.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the first article (maqala) of the book, which deals with Porphyry's Isagoge (a classic introduction to logic). The author lists ten chapters that will follow. The first chapter covers the divisions of verbal signification (dalalat al-lafz). The second deals with simple and compound expressions. The third further divides simple terms into noun, verb, and particle. The fourth discusses the relation of names to their meanings. The fifth divides simple into universal and particular. The sixth divides the universal into essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi). The seventh divides the essential into genus (jins), species (naw'), and differentia (fasl). The eighth divides the accidental into property (khassa) and general accident (arad 'amm). The ninth enumerates the five universals—genus, species, differentia, property, general accident—and examines previous discussions. The tenth discusses what these five share and differ in. This structure follows the traditional curriculum of logic after the Isagoge.

The Ten Chapters of the First Article on Isagoge
Author enumerates the ten chapters of the first article on Isagoge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical progression of the ten chapters of the first article on Isagoge and explain how each chapter builds on the previous.

Core Idea
The ten chapters follow a logical path from utterance signification to the five universals and their relations.

The first article of this logic work is structured as a sequence of ten chapters that systematically build from the most basic linguistic elements to the complex theory of the five universals. Understanding this progression is essential for grasping the overall method of Islamic logic, which begins with language and moves to conceptual categorization. This lesson will guide you through the order and rationale of each chapter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting point
The list begins with 'divisions of significations of the utterance' (Chapter One). Why start here? Logic in the Islamic tradition is rooted in language. All reasoning is expressed through utterances, so we must first understand how words signify meanings.
2
Trace the movement from simple to compound
Chapter Two distinguishes between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) expressions. This is a fundamental dichotomy in Arabic grammar and logic. The simple is then further divided into noun, verb, and particle in Chapter Three.
Note that the order follows a logical analysis: first the whole, then its parts.
3
Follow the transition from linguistic to logical
Chapters Four and Five bridge language and logic: Chapter Four examines how nouns relate to their meanings, and Chapter Five divides the simple into universal (kulli) and particular (juzʾi). This introduces the key conceptual distinction that underlies the rest of the article.
4
Map the divisions of universals
Chapters Six through Eight break down the universal: first into essential (dhati) and accidental (ʿaradi) in Chapter Six, then essential into genus (jins), species (nawʿ), and differentia (fasl) in Chapter Seven, and accidental into property (khassa) and general accident (ʿarad ʿamm) in Chapter Eight.
This is the core content of the Isagoge: the five universals are genus, species, differentia, property, and accidental.
5
Observe the summary and synthesis
Chapters Nine and Ten conclude by encompassing all five universals and analyzing their commonalities and differences. This mirrors the classical structure of Porphyry's Isagoge, ending with a comparative study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الأولى في إيساغوجي وتشتمل على عشرة فصول: الفصل الأول: في أقسام دلالات اللفظ. الفصل الثاني: في المفرد والمركب. الفصل الثالث: في تحقيق قسمة المفرد إلى الاسم والكلمة والأداة. الفصل الرابع: في تحقيق نسبة الأسماء إلى معانيها. الفصل الخامس: في تحقيق قسمة المفرد إلى الكلي والجزئي. الفصل السادس: في قسمة الكلي إلى الذاتي والعرضي. الفصل السابع: في قسمة الذاتي إلى الجنس والنوع والفصل. الفصل الثامن في قسمة العرضي إلى الخاصة والعرض العام. الفصل التاسع: في حصر الكليات الخمس وتتبع ما قيل فيه. الفصل العاشر: في بيان ما تشترك فيه الكليات الخمس، وتفترق وتحقيق ما قيل فيه.
The first article on Isagoge, and it includes ten chapters: Chapter One: On the divisions of significations of the utterance. Chapter Two: On the simple and the compound. Chapter Three: On verifying the division of the simple into noun, verb, and particle. Chapter Four: On verifying the relation of nouns to their meanings. Chapter Five: On verifying the division of the simple into universal and particular. Chapter Six: On the division of the universal into essential and accidental. Chapter Seven: On the division of the essential into genus, species, and differentia. Chapter Eight: On the division of the accidental into property and general accident. Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the table of contents for the first article of the book, which is devoted to the Isagoge—the introductory logical work on the five universal terms (al-kulliyyat al-khams). The author lists ten chapters, each building upon the previous. The sequence begins with the basic elements of logic: the signification of utterances (dalalat al-lafz), then progresses to the distinction between simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab) expressions. From there, it delves into the grammatical categories of noun, verb, and particle, then the relationship between names and their meanings. The pivotal division of terms into universal (kulli) and particular (juzʾi) is introduced, followed by the essential (dhati) versus accidental (ʿaradi) classification. Within the essentials, the author enumerates the three key universals: genus (jins), species (nawʿ), and differentia (fasl). For accidents, he includes property (khassa) and general accident (ʿarad ʿamm). Finally, the last two chapters offer a comprehensive enumeration of all five universals and an analysis of their commonalities and differences. This structure mirrors the classical organization of Porphyry's Isagoge, which was standard in Islamic logic curricula.
