Why Words Must Be Studied Before Logic
Author justifies the need to study words before explicatory statements and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical and pedagogical necessity of studying words before compound logical expressions.

Core Idea
The composite cannot be known without first knowing its simple parts.

In this passage, the author establishes the foundational order of logic. He argues that since logical investigations concern composite meanings—explicatory statements and proofs—and these are built from simple words, we must first study the parts (words) before the wholes. This mirrors how all Islamic sciences approach their subjects: from simple to complex.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of logical expressions
The author mentions 'explicatory statements' (al-aqwal al-shariha) and 'proofs' (hujaj). These are the composite meanings that logic investigates.
2
Recognize the role of teaching and dialogue
Logic is difficult and must be taught through speech and dialogue. This necessitates understanding the medium of communication: words.
Teaching logic requires using words to convey meanings.
3
Understand the compositional structure
Compound expressions are made of simple words. Just as you cannot know a building without knowing its bricks, you cannot know a compound logical expression without knowing its simple components.
The author states: 'We cannot conceive knowledge of the composite before obtaining knowledge of that from which the composition is made.'
4
Trace the dependence chain
Even the divisions of words (simple/composite, universal/particular) are understood relative to their subject (signification). So we must first study signification itself before word divisions.
This establishes a clear sequence: signification → words → compound expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في أقسام دلالات اللفظ نقول: قد ثبت أن البحث المنطقي من حيث هو منطقي إنما هو في معاني الأقوال الشارحة والحجج، لكنه لما كان هذا القانون قد يستعصي على الطباع إلا بتعلم وتعليم، ولم يمكن ذلك إلا بالمخاطبة والمحاورة كما سبق، دعت الحاجة إلى النظر في أحوال الألفاظ من جهة دلالتها، وتحقيق أقسامها، ولما كانت معاني الأقوال الشارحة والحجج مؤلفة، ولها مفردات تكون ائتلافها منها، كانت الأقوال الدالة عليها أيضاً مؤلفة وما يدل به على المفردات معانيها هي مفرداتها، ولن نتصور العلم بالمؤلف قبل حصول العلم بما منه التأليف، فلا جرم أن نقدم النظر في مفردات الألفاظ وأوضاعها وتحقيق أقسامها أولاً، ولما كانت معرفة أقسام اللفظ مفردة ومركبة وكليّة وجزئية، وغير ذلك من أقسامه، إنما يعرف بالنسبة إلى موضوعه، ولن نتصور ذلك قبل النظر في نفس الدلالة وأقسامها، لم يتسع الحال إلا لتقديمها.
Chapter One: On the Divisions of the Significations of the Word. We say: It has been established that logical investigation, insofar as it is logical, is only about the meanings of explicatory statements and proofs. But since this discipline may be difficult for natures except through learning and teaching, and that is not possible except through address and dialogue as previously mentioned, the need arose to consider the states of words in terms of their signification and to verify their divisions. And since the meanings of explicatory statements and proofs are composite, and they have simple parts from which their composition arises, the statements indicating them are also composite, and that by which the simple meanings are indicated are their simple parts. We cannot conceive knowledge of the composite before obtaining knowledge of that from which the composition is made. Therefore, we must first present the consideration of simple words, their uses, and the verification of their divisions. And since knowledge of the divisions of the word—simple and composite, universal and particular, and other divisions—is only known in relation to its subject, and we cannot conceive that before considering signification itself and its divisions, the situation allowed only for presenting it.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to the study of logic by justifying the need to first examine words and their signification. The author argues that logical inquiry focuses on the meanings of explicatory statements (al-aqwal al-shariha) and proofs (hujaj). However, because this discipline is difficult and must be taught through speech and dialogue, we must first understand the instruments of communication: words. Since compound expressions are built from simple ones, knowing the parts precedes knowing the whole. Similarly, the divisions of words (simple/composite, universal/particular) are understood relative to their subject matter, which itself depends on understanding signification. Thus, the author establishes a foundational order: study simple words, then their significations, then the divisions of words, before tackling compound logical expressions. This mirrors the pedagogical approach in Islamic logic, where the study of lafz (word) and its dalala (signification) precedes syllogistic reasoning.

الأقوال الشارحة
الحجج
مفردات
مركبات

Key Takeaway
The study of logic must begin with simple words and their signification because compound logical expressions are built from them.

Why Logic Begins with Words: The Methodological Justification
Author explains why logical study must begin with verbal expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument for starting logic with verbal signification (al-dalala), explaining why simple expressions must precede composite ones.

Core Idea
Meanings are learned through words, so words come first.

The author of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq has established that logic investigates the meanings of definitions and proofs. But now he asks: why begin with words? In this passage, he offers a systematic justification rooted in the nature of teaching and the structure of composite meaning. This is not a detour—it is the necessary foundation for all logical inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subjects of logic
The author states that logical investigation is ‘only about the meanings of explicative statements and proofs.’ These two are the core subjects of logic. Notice that he does not mention words at first.
Explicative statements (al-aqwal al-shariha) are definitions; proofs (al-hijaj) are arguments.
2
Spot the practical obstacle
The law of logic is hard for natural temperaments to grasp except through learning and teaching, which requires speech and dialogue. This is the key practical reason: we must use language to teach logic.
This links back to the earlier discussion of teaching as essential for logic.
3
Trace the argument from composite to simple
Meanings of definitions and proofs are composite. Composite meanings are built from simple parts. The expressions signifying those meanings are also composite. We cannot know a composite without knowing its components, so we must first study simple expressions.
This is an epistemological principle: knowledge of the whole depends on knowledge of its parts.
4
Realize the role of signification (dalala)
To study expressions, we must understand their divisions (simple/composite, universal/particular). But those divisions are understood only relative to the subject of signification itself. So we must first consider dalala and its types.
Thus the entire chain: logic → definitions/proofs (composite meanings) → simple meanings → expressions → signification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد ثبت أن البحث المنطقي من حيث هو منطقي إنما هو في معاني الأقوال الشارحة والحجج، لكنه لما كان هذا القانون قد يستعصي على الطباع إلا بتعلم وتعليم، ولم يمكن ذلك إلا بالمخاطبة والمحاورة كما سبق، دعت الحاجة إلى النظر في أحوال الألفاظ من جهة دلالتها، وتحقيق أقسامها، ولما كانت معاني الأقوال الشارحة والحجج مؤلفة، ولها مفردات تكون ائتلافها منها، كانت الأقوال الدالة عليها أيضاً مؤلفة وما يدل به على المفردات معانيها هي مفرداتها، ولن نتصور العلم بالمؤلف قبل حصول العلم بما منه التأليف، فلا جرم أن نقدم النظر في مفردات الألفاظ وأوضاعها وتحقيق أقسامها أولاً، ولما كانت معرفة أقسام اللفظ مفردة ومركبة وكليّة وجزئية، وغير ذلك من أقسامه، إنما يعرف بالنسبة إلى موضوعه، ولن نتصور ذلك قبل النظر في نفس الدلالة وأقسامها، لم يتسع الحال إلا لتقديمها.
It has been established that logical investigation, as such, is only about the meanings of explicative statements and proofs. But since this law [of logic] may be refractory to natures except through learning and teaching, and that cannot be done except through address and dialogue as previously mentioned, the need arose to consider the states of expressions with respect to their signification and to investigate their divisions. And since the meanings of explicative statements and proofs are composite and have simple parts from which their composition arises, the expressions signifying them are also composite, and what signifies the simple parts of their meanings are their simple expressions. And we cannot conceive knowledge of the composite before obtaining knowledge of that from which the composition arises. Hence we must first consider simple expressions, their conventions, and the investigation of their divisions. And since knowledge of the divisions of an expression – simple and composite, universal and particular, etc. – is only known relative to its subject, and we cannot conceive that before considering signification itself and its divisions, the situation allowed no alternative but to present it [first].
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage provides the methodological justification for studying verbal signification at the beginning of a logic text. The author argues that although logic proper deals with meanings—specifically the meanings of explicative statements (al-aqwal al-shariha) and proofs (al-hijaj)—the study of those meanings cannot proceed without first understanding the expressions that convey them, because teaching and learning occur through language. Since composite meanings are built from simple components, and expressions are composed in corresponding ways, one must first understand simple expressions and their significations. Furthermore, understanding the divisions of expressions (such as simple/composite, universal/particular) depends on understanding the nature of signification (dalala) itself. Therefore, the author resolves to present the subject of signification first. This is a typical introduction to the section on verbal signification in logical works.

الدلالة
اللفظ
الأقوال الشارحة

Key Takeaway
The study of verbal signification is epistemologically necessary for logic because composite meanings are only known through their simple components, which are conveyed by words.

Structure of the Second Article on Explanatory Statements
Author outlines the second article's structure and key concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two chapters of the second article and distinguish between the restrictive compound and the predicative compound as the only useful types of compounds for definitions.

Core Idea
Explanatory statements are definitional compounds; they are only two types: restrictive and predicative.

This passage marks the beginning of the second major section of the work, which focuses on explanatory statements – the compounds used to define and describe concepts. Just as a builder needs to know the types of bricks before constructing a building, a logician must understand the two fundamental types of definitional compounds. The author outlines the structure of this article, which will cover both the correct types of compounds and common errors in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the article's overall topic
The article is about 'explanatory statements' (al-aqwāl al-shāriḥa). These are compound expressions used to clarify or define a concept. Ask yourself: why would logic need a whole article on this?
2
Recognize the two chapters
The author divides the article into two chapters. The first chapter deals with which compounds are beneficial (nāfi‘) for attaining sought objectives (maṭlūbāt), and the second deals with defective definitions (al-ḥudūd al-fāsida).
Chapter 1 is constructive, chapter 2 is corrective.
3
Note the two types of beneficial compounds
The first chapter states that the beneficial compounds are restricted to two: the restrictive compound (al-murakkab al-taqyīdī) and the predicative compound (al-murakkab al-khabarī). Understand why only these two: one qualifies the subject (e.g., 'rational animal'), the other makes a claim (e.g., 'Zayd is standing').
These correspond to a definition and a proposition respectively.
4
Understand the priority of the restrictive compound
The author mentions that consideration of the restrictive compound comes before the predicative compound. This is because definitions (taqrīr of ma‘nā) are prior to judgments (taṣdīq). In Islamic logic, conceptualization (taṣawwur) precedes assent (taṣdīq).
This ordering reflects the cognitive process: first know the thing, then judge about it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثانية في الأقوال الشارحة وتشتمل على فصلين الفصل الأول: في بيان ما هو النافع من المركبات في تحصيل المطلوبات، وانحصاره في المركب التقييدي والخبري، ووجه تقدم النظر في التقييدي منه على الخبري، وبيان أقسامه وتركيبه. الفصل الثاني: في تعريف الحدود الفاسدة والتنبيه على مواضيع الخلل فيها.
The second article on explanatory statements, and it comprises two chapters. The first chapter: on explaining what is beneficial among compounds in attaining sought objectives, and its restriction to the restrictive compound and the predicative compound, and the aspect of priority of consideration of the restrictive over the predicative, and explanation of its divisions and composition. The second chapter: on defining defective definitions and alerting to the places of error in them.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the second article of the work, which deals with explanatory statements (al-aqwāl al-shāriḥa). The author informs us that this article consists of two chapters. The first chapter will explain which types of compounds are useful for attaining sought-after objectives (maṭlūbāt), and that these useful compounds are limited to two types: the restrictive compound (al-murakkab al-taqyīdī) and the predicative compound (al-murakkab al-khabarī). Furthermore, it will discuss why consideration of the restrictive compound precedes that of the predicative compound, and it will also explain the divisions and composition of these compounds. The second chapter will focus on defining defective definitions (al-ḥudūd al-fāsida) and alerting the student to the points where errors occur in them. This introduction sets the structure for the entire article, much like a table of contents. In the Islamic logical tradition, the study of definitions (ḥadd) and descriptions (rasm) is central to attaining conceptual knowledge (taṣawwur), so understanding the different types of compounds is a foundational step.

Key Terms

المركب التقييدي
المركب الخبري
الحدود الفاسدة

Four Modes of Examining Hadd and Rasm
Author introduces the four modes of examining hadd and rasm and postpones acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four modes of examining hadd and rasm and explain why acquisition is postponed.

Core Idea
Hadd and rasm can be examined under four lenses: acquisition, composition, existence, and corruption – but acquisition depends on arguments.

This lesson introduces the structure of the first chapter of the text. The author presents four ways to analyze a definition (hadd) or description (rasm): how it is acquired (iktisab), how it is composed (ta'lif), whether it exists under required conditions (wujud), and how it can be corrupted (fasad). However, one of these – acquisition – is postponed because it relies on arguments that have not yet been covered. Understanding this framing helps you navigate the logical progression of the work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four modes
Extract from the passage the four aspects of examining hadd and rasm: acquisition (iktisab), composition (ta'lif), existence (wujud), and corruption (fasad). Notice that the author lists them in a specific order.
The fourth mode combines existence and corruption.
2
Understand the postponement of acquisition
The author states that acquisition is tied to arguments (hujaj), which have not yet been discussed. Therefore, it is delayed until after the section on arguments.
The author acknowledges this postponement is pedagogical, not logical.
3
Recognize the pedagogical strategy
Realize that the author prioritizes the more difficult parts (hadd and rasm) and postpones acquisition to avoid overburdening the reader before arguments are introduced.
This reflects a common teaching method in Islamic logic: building foundational concepts first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان اللفظي سهل التناول على قاصديه فربما كره الأطناب فيه، وكان الواجب صرف العناية إلى تحقيق القسمين الأولين لعسرهما، وعند ذلك نقول الحد والرس قد ينظر إليهما تارة من جهة اكتسابهما، وأخرى من جهة تأليفهما، وتارة [ثالثة] من جهة وجودهما على الحال المشروط فيهما، وكيفية تأليفهما، وتارة من جهة وجودهما وفسادهما. أما النظر في جهة اكتسابهما: فإنه لما كان له نوع تعلق بالحجج، ولم نأت على بيانها بعد، كان الواجب تأخيره إلى حين ما نوضح الكلام في الحجج، وإن كان اللائق أن يكون هاهنا.
Since the verbal (lafdhi) is easy to grasp for its seekers, perhaps they dislike prolixity in it, and the duty is to direct attention to verifying the first two divisions because of their difficulty. At that point we say: hadd and rasm are sometimes considered from the perspective of their acquisition, other times from the perspective of their composition, and a third time from the perspective of their existence in the condition required in them and the manner of their composition, and other times from the perspective of their existence and corruption. As for the consideration of their acquisition: since it has some connection with arguments (hujaj), and we have not yet explained them, it is necessary to postpone it until the time when we clarify the discussion of arguments, although it would be fitting to include it here.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to the first chapter on beneficial compounds. The author states that since the lafdhi definition is easy, they will focus on the harder types: hadd and rasm. He then outlines four ways to examine them: (1) acquisition (iktisab) — how one arrives at them, (2) composition (ta'lif) — how they are constructed, (3) existence (wujud) under the required conditions and proper composition, and (4) their corruption (fasad). However, the perspective of acquisition is deferred because it relates to arguments (hujaj), which have not yet been discussed. The author acknowledges that logically it would belong here, but he postpones it for pedagogical reasons. This reveals the structure of the work: first definitions, then arguments.

The Two Arts of the Third Article: Categorical and Conditional
Author introduces the third article on Parmenides and divides it into two arts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish categorical from conditional propositions and provide an Islamic science example of each.

Core Idea
Knowledge through assent rests on two proposition types: categorical and conditional.

This lesson opens the third major section of the book, which is dedicated to decisive statements that produce knowledge. The author immediately classifies these statements into two fundamental types: categorical and conditional. Understanding this division is crucial because it shapes how logicians and scholars in Islamic sciences construct and evaluate arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the third article's subject
The passage states that the third article deals with 'decisive statements' (al-aqwal al-jazima) that 'indicate knowledge' (dalla 'ala al-'ulum). This sets the stage for all subsequent logical analysis.
Remember that 'decisive' means truth-apt statements capable of assent.
2
Identify the first art: categorical propositions
The first art is about categorical propositions (qadaya hamliyya). These are simple statements with a subject and predicate, without any condition. For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is categorical because it directly attributes a predicate to a subject.
Categorical propositions are the building blocks of definition and deduction.
3
Identify the second art: conditional propositions
The second art concerns conditional propositions (qadaya shartiyya), which include a condition connecting two parts. Classic examples are conditionals ('If the sun rises, then it is day') and disjunctives ('Either the act is wajib or mubah').
Conditionals often appear in legal reasoning and theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في بارمنياس وهي الأقوال الجازمة الدالة على العلوم وتشتمل على فنين التصديقية الفن الأول في القضايا الحَمْلية. الفن الثاني في القضايا الشرطية.
The third article on Parmenides, and it is the decisive statements that indicate knowledge, and it comprises two arts: the assertive. The first art is on categorical propositions. The second art is on conditional propositions.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a formal introduction to the third major section (article) of the book, which is titled 'Parmenides'. The author defines this article as dealing with 'decisive statements' (al-aqwal al-jazima) that indicate knowledge (al-'ulum). He then divides it into two arts (fannan): the first art concerns categorical propositions (qadaya hamliyya), which are statements about a subject and predicate without condition (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'). The second art concerns conditional propositions (qadaya shartiyya), which involve a condition (e.g., 'If the sun rises, then it is day'). The term 'assertive' (tasdiqiyya) likely characterizes the nature of these statements as yielding assent. This division is foundational for understanding the logical structure of the work.

Key Terms
قضايا حَمْلية
قضايا شرطية
تصديقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Three arts: categorical, conditional, and disjunctive propositions

Key Takeaway
The third article divides the study of decisive statements into categorical and conditional propositions, forming the two pillars of logical analysis in this work.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Eight Chapters on Categorical Propositions
Enumeration of the eight chapters of the first art on categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and describe the eight chapters of the first art on categorical propositions as presented in the source.

Core Idea
Eight systematic chapters build the science of categorical propositions.

This lesson maps the entire first art of the work, which deals with categorical propositions. You will see how the author organizes topics from basic concepts to advanced relations like conversion and contradiction. Understanding this structure helps you navigate the logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main subject
The first art is introduced as 'الفن الأول في القضايا الحملية' (the first art on categorical propositions). Note that 'حملیة' means categorical, as opposed to conditional.
2
List the eight chapters
The passage enumerates eight chapters (فصول). Write them down in order: 1) manner of investigation, 2) divisions by subject, 3) deviation and error, 4) divisions by predicate (simple/modified/privative), 5) matters and modalities, 6) relation of predicate to subject, 7) contradiction, 8) conversion.
3
Understand the logical progression
Notice the movement: first foundation (ch1-2), then issues and classifications (ch3-4), then deeper analysis of propositions (ch5-6), and finally key logical operations (ch7-8).
4
Identify key terms per chapter
For example, chapter four introduces 'بسيطة' (simple), 'معدولة' (modified), 'عدمية' (privative). Chapter seven is 'تناقض' (contradiction). Chapter eight is 'تعاكس' (conversion).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول في القضايا الحملية ويشتمل على ثمانية فصول: الفصل الأول في وجه تقديم النظر في الحَمْلي، وتحقيق الحَمْلية، وبيان مفرداتها. الفصل الثاني: في تعريف أقسام القضية باعتبار موضوعها. الفصل الثالث في ما يلحق القضية الحَمْلية من التحريف والغلط وما يُظن به التحريف وليس منحرفا. الفصل الرابع في انقسام القضية الحَمْلية باعتبار محمولها إلى البسيطة، والمعدولة والعدمية، وبيان أحكامها من العموم والخصوص والتقابل. الفصل الخامس: في مواد القضايا وجهاتها وإطلاقها، وتلازمها وتعادها. الفصل السادس: في تحقيق نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع. الفصل السابع: في التناقض. الفصل الثامن في التعاكس.
The first art: on categorical propositions. It comprises eight chapters: Chapter one: on the manner of presenting the investigation of the categorical, and the verification of the categorical, and the explanation of its terms. Chapter two: on defining the divisions of the proposition with respect to its subject. Chapter three: on what attaches to the categorical proposition of deviation and error, and what is thought to be deviation but is not deviant. Chapter four: on the division of the categorical proposition with respect to its predicate into the simple, the modified, and the privative, and the explanation of their rulings of generality, specificity, and opposition. Chapter five: on the matters of propositions, their modalities, their absoluteness, their mutual implications, and their mutual contradictoriness. Chapter six: on the verification of the relation of the predicate to the subject. Chapter seven: on contradiction. Chapter eight: on conversion.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the table of contents for the first art (الفن الأول) of the work, which deals with categorical propositions (القضايا الحملية). The author lists eight chapters (فصول) that systematically cover the subject. The first chapter lays the groundwork by discussing how to approach the investigation of categorical propositions (الحَمْلي), verifying the nature of the categorical (الحَمْلية), and explaining its constituent terms (مفرداتها). The second chapter defines the divisions of propositions based on the subject (الموضوع). The third chapter addresses accidental deviations and errors that affect categorical propositions, as well as cases that appear deviant but are not. The fourth chapter divides categorical propositions based on the predicate (المحمول) into three types: simple (البسيطة), modified (المعدولة), and privative (العدمية), and explains their rulings concerning generality, specificity, and opposition. The fifth chapter discusses the matter (مواد) of propositions, their modalities (جهات), absoluteness (إطلاق), mutual implications (تلازم), and mutual contradictions (تعاند). The sixth chapter verifies the relation (نسبة) of the predicate to the subject. The seventh chapter deals with contradiction (التناقض) — likely referring to the square of opposition. The eighth chapter covers conversion (التعاكس) — the operation of swapping subject and predicate. This outline shows a comprehensive treatment of categorical propositions, moving from basic concepts to complex logical relations.

الحَمْلي
المعدولة

Roadmap of Conditional Propositions
Author outlines the three chapters on conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the three chapters on conditional propositions and identify the key topics in each.

Core Idea
Conditional logic is built on three pillars: division, composition, and properties.

Before diving into the details of conditional propositions, the author provides a roadmap. This lesson helps you navigate the upcoming chapters so you know what to expect and how the pieces fit together. Understanding this structure will prevent confusion later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main topic
Locate the phrase 'الفن الثاني: القضايا الشرطية'. This tells you the overall subject: conditional propositions.
2
Count the chapters
The text says 'ثلاثة فصول' – three chapters. List them in order: الفصل الأول, الفصل الثاني, الفصل الثالث.
3
Extract the focus of each chapter
For each chapter, note the key terms: الأول: division into متصلة and منفصلة; الثاني: composition (تأليف), deviation (المنحرفة), agreement/difference; الثالث: quantified types and rulings (التلازم, التناقض, التعاكس).
4

وتشتمل على ثلاثة فصول:
الفصل الأول في بيان انقسام الشرطية إلى المتصلة والمنفصلة،
وتحقيق كل واحدة منها.
الفصل الثاني في تعريف تأليف القضايا الشرطية، وما تألف منه
وكيفية المنحرفة منها، وما به اتفاقها و افتراقها.
الفصل الثالث: في تعريف أحوال المخصوصات والمهملات والمحصورات من الشرطيات، وأحكامها من التلازم والتناقض
والتعاكس.
The second art: Conditional propositions. And it includes three chapters: The first chapter: On explaining the division of conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive, and verification of each of them. The second chapter: On defining the composition of conditional propositions, what they are composed of, the manner of deviating from them, and what they agree and differ in. The third chapter: On defining the states of particularized, absolute, and quantified conditional propositions, and their rulings regarding implication, contradiction, and conversion.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an organizational overview, introducing the second main topic (الفن الثاني) of the book: conditional propositions (القضايا الشرطية). It lists three chapters that will follow. The first chapter studies the division of conditional propositions into conjunctive (المتصلة) and disjunctive (المنفصلة), and the verification of each type. The second chapter defines the composition of conditional propositions, their constituents, how they deviate (المنحرفة), and points of agreement and difference. The third chapter covers particularized (المخصوصات), absolute (المهملات), and quantified (المحصورات) conditionals, and their properties of implication (التلازم), contradiction (التناقض), and conversion (التعاكس). This is a typical table of contents in classical works, providing a roadmap for the student.

Key Takeaway
The study of conditionals is organized into three logical steps: categories, composition, and formal properties.

First Rule on Categorical Syllogisms: Chapter Overview
Author enumerates the seven chapters of the first rule on categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven chapters of the first rule on categorical syllogisms and describe the focus of each.

Core Idea
The first rule is structured into seven chapters, from definitions to figure verification.

This passage serves as a roadmap for the first rule, which deals with categorical syllogisms. The author does not dive into content immediately but first lays out the chapter structure. Understanding this overview will help you see how the detailed discussions later are organized.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall topic
The first rule is about categorical syllogisms (al-iqtirānāt al-ḥamliyya). Recognize that this is the main subject of the seven chapters.
2
Count the chapters
The text says 'وتشتمل على سبعة فصول' (it contains seven chapters). Confirm the number.
3
Summarize each chapter's focus
Chapter 1 covers divisions of arguments and definition of syllogism. Chapter 2 divides syllogism into conjunctive and conditional. Chapters 3-6 each verify one of the four figures and their conditions. Chapter 7 compares the four figures.
This sequence moves from general to specific, then to comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الأولى
في الاقترانات الحملية
وتشتمل على سبعة فصول:
الفصل الأول: في أقسام الحجج، وحد القياس، وتتبع ما قيل فيه.
الفصل الثاني: في انقسام القياس إلى الاقتراني والاستثنائي وتحقيق الاقتراني.

The First Rule: On Categorical Conjunctions. And it contains seven chapters: Chapter One: On the divisions of arguments, the definition of syllogism, and a critique of what has been said about it. Chapter Two: On the division of syllogism into conjunctive and conditional, and the verification of the conjunctive. Chapter Three: On the verification of the first figure and its conditions. Chapter Four: On the verification of the second figure and its conditions. Chapter Five: On the verification of the third figure and its conditions. Chapter Six: On the verification of the fourth figure and its conditions. Chapter Seven: On the verification of what the four figures agree upon and differ in.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an introductory outline for the first rule (al-qāʿida al-ūlā) of the book, which deals with categorical syllogisms (al-iqtirānāt al-ḥamliyya). The author lists seven chapters that will follow. Chapter One covers the divisions of arguments (aqsām al-ḥujaj), the definition of syllogism (ḥadd al-qiyās), and a critical survey of what has been said on the topic. Chapter Two divides syllogism into conjunctive (iqtirānī) and conditional (istithnāʾī) and then focuses on verifying the conjunctive. Chapters Three through Six each verify one of the four figures (al-ashkāl al-arbaʿa) and their conditions (qarāʾin). The seventh chapter will discuss what all four figures share (tattafiq) and where they differ (taftariq). This structure is typical of logical works: first define and classify, then treat each figure individually, and finally compare them.

Identifying a Colophon in an Islamic Manuscript
Colophon marking end of volume one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a colophon in an Arabic manuscript and explain its function as a volume marker.

Core Idea
The colophon is the manuscript's signature – it marks completion, heritage, and hope.

This final passage of the first volume of Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq is a classic colophon. It announces the end of this part and the promise of a second part, accompanied by the formula 'in sha' Allah' and praise to Allah. Recognizing colophons is essential for navigating and understanding Islamic manuscripts.

Guided Path
1
Read the colophon phrase
Look at the Arabic: 'هذا الجزء ويليه الجزء الثاني إن شاء الله تعالى والحمد لله رب العالمين'. This is the closing statement of the volume.
Colophons often include the phrase 'هذا الجزء' (this part) or 'تم الكتاب' (the book is completed).
2
Identify the volume marker
Note that it explicitly says 'الجزء الأول' is ending and 'الجزء الثاني' will follow. This tells you the work is structured in multiple volumes.
Sometimes colophons also include the date and scribe's name.
3
Understand the pious formulas
The phrases 'إن شاء الله' and 'الحمد لله رب العالمين' are standard Islamic expressions of humility and gratitude. They are not part of the content but frame the completion.
These formulas are typical and help date manuscripts culturally.
4
Recognize the manuscript tradition
Become aware that such colophons are not part of the author's original text but were added by scribes or publishers to mark the end of a volume or copy.
In critical editions, these are usually noted but not part of the main text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الجزء ويليه الجزء الثاني إن شاء الله تعالى والحمد لله رب العالمين
This is the part, and it will be followed by the second part, God willing, and praise be to God, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This is a colophon indicating the end of the first part (الجزء الأول) of the book. The author notes that the second part will follow, if God wills. It ends with the traditional formula of praise to God. This is a standard closing for a volume in the Islamic manuscript tradition.

Key Terms
هذا الجزء
الجزء الثاني
إن شاء الله تعالى
الحمد لله رب العالمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The author's death
B) The end of the first volume and promise of a second
C) The start of a new chapter
D) A list of corrections

Key Takeaway
A colophon is a scribal marker that signals the end of a manuscript volume, often including pious formulas and promises of continuation, and is essential for understanding a text's transmission.

The Architecture of Logic in Daqaiq al-Haqa'iq
Table of contents of the first volume on logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the logical sequence of topics in al-Amidi's Daqaiq al-Haqa'iq and explain why the Isagoge is the foundational article.

Core Idea
Every classical logic text begins with the five universals to equip the student for all that follows.

This passage is the table of contents for the first volume of al-Amidi's Daqaiq al-Haqa'iq. It reveals the logical structure of the entire work, beginning with two introductory chapters (on the need for logic and its subject matter), then the first article on Isagoge (Porphyry's Introduction), which itself starts with the divisions of signification and culminates in the five universals. Understanding this blueprint helps you see how classical Islamic logic texts are systematically built.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two introductions
The table of contents shows 'First introduction' and 'Second introduction' before the first article. These cover the enumeration of sciences, the necessity of logic, and its purpose and subject matter.
These introductions establish the epistemological importance of logic.
2
Identify the first article and its main theme
The first article is 'On Isagoge'. Why does the book start with this? Because the Isagoge introduces the five predicables (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) which are the building blocks of definitions.
Isagoge is the Greek term for 'introduction'.
3
Trace the chapter sequence within the Isagoge
The chapters move from signification (dalalat) to simple/compound, parts of speech, relation of words to meanings, universal/particular, essential/accidental, and finally the division of essential into genus, species, and differentia.
This is a logical progression from language to concepts to the most basic classification of universals.
4
Understand why this sequence matters
The Isagoge provides the foundational concepts needed for all later discussions in logic, such as definition, division, and syllogism. Without mastering the five universals, one cannot evaluate definitions or understand categorical propositions.
This structure is standard in classical Islamic logic texts.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the first volume of Daqaiq al-Haqa'iq by Sayf al-Din al-Amidi. It provides an overview of the book's structure, beginning with a preface on the author's life and methodology, then moving into the main subject: logic. The logic section is divided into two introductions and one article. The first introduction covers the necessity of logic among the sciences, while the second explains the need for logic, its purpose, and its subject matter. The first article is on Isagoge (Porphyry's introduction), which includes chapters on signification, simple and compound terms, the parts of speech, the relation of expressions to meanings, the division into universal and particular, the division of universal into essential and accidental, and finally the division of essential into genus, species, and differentia. This table of contents serves as a roadmap for the reader, showing that the book follows the classical sequence of logic texts starting with the Isagoge.

Key Terms
إيساغوجي (Isaghuji): Refers to Porphyry's Isagoge, the introductory work on the five predicables (genus, species, differentia, property, accident). [Note: In later texts, Isaghuji often refers to the entire field of introductory logic; here it is the specific work.]
منطق (mantiq): The science of reasoning that protects the mind from error in thinking. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
ذاتي (dhati): Essential; what is predicated of a thing as part of its quiddity. [Note: In later logic, dhati is contrasted with 'aradi (accidental); here it is a division of the universal.]

Key Takeaway
The table of contents reveals that al-Amidi follows the classical order: from the need for logic, to the Isagoge on the five universals, laying the groundwork for all subsequent logical analysis.

Parsing the Table of Contents of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq
Table of contents for volume one of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to navigate the first volume of al-Amidi's Daqaiq al-Haqaiq by identifying the three main articles and their chapter topics.

Core Idea
A table of contents is a logical roadmap; mastering it reveals the author's pedagogical strategy.

This lesson helps you understand the structure of al-Amidi's logic text. By parsing its table of contents, you will see how he organizes the study of logic: beginning with the Isagoge (universals), moving to definitions, and then to categorical propositions. This will orient you for deeper study.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three articles
Look for 'المقالة الأولى', 'المقالة الثانية', 'المقالة الثالثة'. These are the main divisions of the volume. Each article covers a major topic in logic.
The first article is on Isagoge, the second on definitions, the third on categorical propositions.
2
Examine the chapters under each article
Count the chapters and note their titles. The first article has 10 chapters, the second has 2, and the third's first genre has 4. This shows the emphasis on the introductory material.
The third article also has a second genre (not listed here) on conditional propositions.
3
Observe the logical progression
The text moves from simple (signification of words) to complex (propositions). Definitions (article 2) build on universals (article 1), and categorical propositions (article 3) use the terms defined earlier.
This progression reflects the standard order in Islamic logic texts.
4
Use the table of contents as a reference tool
When studying a specific topic, refer back to this map to see its context. For example, the chapter on the five universals (الفصل التاسع) is part of the first article, which sets the foundation for definitions.
This helps you understand why certain topics are placed where they are.

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for the first volume of Sayf al-Din al-Amidi's 'Daqaiq al-Haqaiq', a major work in Islamic logic. It outlines the structure of the volume: it begins with an introduction by the editor and a biographical note on al-Amidi, followed by the author's own introduction. The first science treated is logic. The text is divided into prefaces and articles. The first preface discusses the classification of sciences and why logic should be studied first. The second preface explains the need for logic, its purpose, and its subject matter. Then follows the first article on Isagoge, which covers chapters on signification, singular and compound words, parts of speech, the relation of words to meanings, division into universal/particular, essential/accidental, genus/species/differentia, property/accident, the five universals, and how they agree/differ. The second article deals with explanatory statements (definitions), with chapters on useful compounds and invalid definitions. The third article is on 'Bariminias' (Categories), which are categorical propositions. This article includes chapters on categorical propositions, their divisions by subject, quantifier-related errors, and divisions by predicate type (simple, modified, privative). The table of contents serves as a roadmap for the reader, indicating that the volume covers the basics of logic following the traditional Isagoge structure, then moves to definition theory, and finally to categorical propositions.

Navigating the Structure of Daqaiq Haqaiq
Lists the chapters and sections of the logic text Daqaiq Haqaiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four articles (maqalat) and their branches (funun) in the table of contents of Daqaiq Haqaiq and locate specific topics such as categorical propositions and syllogisms.

Core Idea
The table of contents is your map to the logical terrain of Daqaiq Haqaiq.

Logic texts like Daqaiq Haqaiq are carefully structured. Before diving into definitions and syllogisms, you need to know how the book is organized. This table of contents lists every major section—articles, branches, chapters—so you can navigate the text with purpose. In this lesson, you will learn to read this map and understand the logical progression of the book.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three major divisions: articles (maqalat) and their contents.
Look for the headings 'المقالة الأولى', 'المقالة الثانية', etc. Note that the fourth article has branches (funun). The first article covers five universals, second covers explanatory statements, third covers propositions (Peri Hermeneias), fourth covers arguments (syllogisms).
The table of contents may have slightly irregular numbering; the fourth article is listed as 'المقالة الرابعة'.
2
Examine the branches (funun) within articles three and four.
Article three has two branches: first on categorical propositions, second on conditional propositions. Article four has one branch on conjunctive syllogisms, with a foundation (qa'ida) on conjunctive categoricals, then chapters on figures.
Note the page numbers—they help locate the discussion.
3
Observe the chapters (fusul) within each branch.
For example, the first branch of article three has eight chapters on categoricals: subject types, distortion, predicate types, modalities, relation, contradiction, conversion, etc. This shows the logical progression from simple propositions to their interrelations.
The order of chapters follows a pedagogical order.
4
Relate the structure to the classical logic curriculum.
The sequence—five universals, definitions (hadd), propositions (qadaya), and syllogisms (qiyas)—is standard in Islamic logic. Recognizing this order helps you understand the logical dependencies: definitions rely on universals, propositions on definitions, and syllogisms on propositions.
This is a typical organization in post-Avicennan logic.

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for the logic text 'Daqaiq Haqaiq'. It lists the major articles, branches, chapters, and sections of the book along with their starting page numbers. The structure shows that the book is divided into articles (maqalat) and branches (funun) covering topics such as the five universals, explanatory statements (definitions), categorical and conditional propositions, contradiction, conversion, and syllogisms. The table of contents serves as a roadmap for the reader to locate specific discussions. For example, the second article deals with 'explanatory statements' (al-aqwal al-shariha), which includes definitions. The third article is 'Peri Hermeneias' (interpretation) covering assertoric statements. The fourth article discusses forms of arguments, including conjunctive syllogisms in the first and second figures. The ellipses and numbers indicate page references. This passage does not contain logical arguments but provides an overview of the book's content.

The Third and Fourth Syllogistic Figures
Table of contents entry for two chapters on syllogistic figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism and distinguish them from the first two figures based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The third figure proves universality of properties; the fourth figure rarely occurs in natural reasoning.

After thoroughly examining the first two figures, the author now turns to the less intuitive but logically significant third and fourth figures. This table of contents entry signals a systematic verification (tahqiq) of each figure and its valid moods (qara'in). The third figure has the middle term as the subject of both premises, while the fourth figure has it as the predicate of the major premise and subject of the minor. Understanding these figures completes your grasp of categorical syllogisms and their application in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the entry's structure
Note that the passage lists two chapters separately: Chapter Five on the third figure, Chapter Six on the fourth. This indicates a systematic division of the subject matter.
The term 'tahqiq' (verification) suggests the author will not merely describe but also confirm the valid forms.
2
Recall the definition of a figure
A figure (shakl) in categorical logic is determined solely by the position of the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) in the two premises.
If the middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor, it's the first figure; predicate of both is second; subject of both is third; predicate of major and subject of minor is fourth.
3
Characterize the third figure
In the third figure, the middle term is the subject of both premises. For example: 'Every B is A; every B is C; therefore some A is C.' This figure is useful for disproving universal statements.
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion, never a universal one.
4
Characterize the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor. Example: 'Every A is B; no B is C; therefore no C is A.' This figure is rarely used in natural reasoning but completes the logical system.
The fourth figure is the least intuitive; some logicians consider it artificial.
5
Understand 'qara'in' as moods
The term 'qara'in' (moods) refers to the valid combinations of quantity and quality of the premises within each figure. For the third figure, there are six valid moods; for the fourth, five.
Learning the moods for each figure helps verify whether a given syllogism is formally valid.

Source Text

Chapter Five: On the Verification of the Third Figure and Its Moods; Chapter Six: On the Verification of the Fourth Figure and Its Moods.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents entry from the book 'Daqaiq Haqaiq'. It announces the upcoming chapters on the third and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism. In classical logic, the syllogism is divided into four figures based on the position of the middle term. The third figure has the middle term as the subject of both premises, while the fourth figure has it as the predicate of the major premise and subject of the minor. The author here states his intention to verify (tahqiq) each figure and enumerate its valid moods (qara'in). This indicates a structured, systematic approach typical of later commentaries.

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth figures complete the four-fold classification of categorical syllogisms, each defined by the placement of the middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid third-figure syllogism on an Islamic legal question. Use the mood Darapti (AII in third figure): All intoxicants are forbidden (khamr); all intoxicants are drinks; therefore some forbidden things are drinks. Verify that the middle term (intoxicants) is the subject in both premises.
?
Take a fourth-figure syllogism: All forbidden things are sins; no sin is beneficial for the afterlife; therefore nothing beneficial for the afterlife is forbidden. Check if the conclusion follows from the premises using the fourth-figure valid mood Camestres.

Introduction to the Second Art: Conditional Syllogisms
Author introduces the second art on conditional syllogisms and its chapters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the structure of the second art on conditional syllogisms and distinguish between productive and sterile syllogisms.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms are a distinct art, but they depend on categorical syllogisms—a key point for Islamic logic.

Welcome to the Second Art of our logic study: Conditional Syllogisms (al-Qiyasat al-Istithna'iyya). This brief passage sets the stage for the next two chapters. We learn that not all conditional syllogisms are valid; some are productive (muntij) and some sterile ('aqim). The second chapter raises an important connection: conditional syllogisms cannot be completed without categorical syllogisms. Let's explore this foundational overview.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope
The author announces the Second Art: 'On Conditional Syllogisms' (في القياسات الاستثنائية). Note that this is a new art, distinct from the previous one on categorical syllogisms.
The Arabic term 'istithna'i' refers to syllogisms that use a 'conditional' premise and then 'state' (istithna') one part.
2
Find the chapters
The passage states it contains two chapters (فصلين). Read the titles: Chapter One 'Explaining the productive and sterile', Chapter Two 'Defining what was said that conditional syllogisms are not completed except with categorical syllogisms'.
Memorize this structure: it outlines the main topics ahead.
3
Define key terms
Productive (muntij) means the syllogism yields a valid conclusion. Sterile ('aqim) means it fails to produce a conclusion. These are crucial for evaluating arguments.
These terms are used throughout the tradition.
4
Recognize the dependency claim
Chapter Two will address a claim that conditional syllogisms need categorical syllogisms to be complete. This shows that the two types are interconnected.
This is a point of debate among logicians.

Source Text

إلا بالاقترانية وتحقيق القول فيه.
The Second Art: On Conditional Syllogisms. It contains two chapters. Chapter One: On explaining the productive and the sterile. Chapter Two: On defining what was said that conditional syllogisms are not completed except with categorical syllogisms, and verifying the statement about that.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents heading. It marks the beginning of the second art (al-fann al-thani) of the book, which treats conditional syllogisms (al-qiyasat al-istithna'iyyat). The author informs that this part comprises two chapters. The first chapter will explain which conditional syllogisms produce a conclusion (al-muntij) and which are sterile (al-'aqim)—that is, which premises yield a valid conclusion and which do not. The second chapter will address a common claim that conditional syllogisms cannot be completed without categorical syllogisms (al-iqtiraniyyat), and will investigate and settle that claim. This sets the stage for the detailed discussions to follow.

Key Terms
القياسات الاستثنائية (al-qiyasat al-istithna'iyya): Syllogisms composed of conditional premises where the conclusion is produced by stating (istithna') one of the parts. [Note: This term is stable across the tradition.]
المنتج (al-muntij): A syllogism that yields a valid conclusion. [Note: Commonly used in logic texts; no significant shift.]
العقيم (al-'aqim): A syllogism that does not produce a conclusion; sterile. [Note: Standard term for invalid syllogisms; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The second art on conditional syllogisms comprises two chapters: one explaining productive vs. sterile, and another examining the relationship between conditional and categorical syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
From the following statements, identify which would be a productive conditional syllogism and which sterile: 'If it rains, the ground is wet. It rained. Therefore, the ground is wet.' vs 'If it rains, the ground is wet. The ground is wet. Therefore, it rained.' (Hint: the second is sterile because affirming the consequent does not produce a valid conclusion.)

Distinguishing Argument Forms for Certainty and Dialectic
Author distinguishes certainty from dialectical argument forms and states their purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between argument forms useful for certainty and those used in dialectic or rhetoric, and explain the purpose of the latter in Islamic scholarly debate.

Core Idea
Not all arguments aim at certainty; dialectic and rhetoric use probable forms to achieve binding consensus.

In the opening of the eighth art, the author makes a critical distinction: some argument forms are not useful for certainty, but that does not make them useless. Instead, they serve a vital role in dialectical and rhetorical contexts, where the goal is persuasion based on widely accepted premises. This lesson will help you understand this distinction and its practical significance in fields like kalam and munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of argument forms
Read the first two lines of the Arabic passage. The author states that some forms are 'not useful for certainty' (غير مفيد لليقين) while implying that others are. Mark this contrast.
Certainty (yaqin) is the domain of demonstration (burhan).
2
Understand the purpose of non-certainty forms
The author says these forms are not a place for error (غلط) in dialectic and rhetoric because their purpose is 'binding in the well-known by what is usually thought' (الإلزام في المشهور بما يظن غالباً). This means they aim to convince using common opinion, not absolute proof.
Dialectic (jadal) and rhetoric (khataba) are two of the five arts (sana'i') in Aristotelian logic.
3
Apply the distinction to Islamic scholarly practice
Consider a kalam debate: a mutakallim often uses premises accepted by the opponent (even if not certain) to establish a theological point. This is precisely the dialectical use of argument forms described here. Identify such a situation in your own study of kalam or usul al-fiqh.

Continuation of the fourth article on the definition of forms of arguments.
Some forms of arguments here are not useful for certainty, and it is something of error that leads to error in demonstration, but it is not a place for error in dialectic and rhetoric; for the purpose of it is only the binding in the well-known by what is usually thought, and it is not falling short of that.
Cause
The eighth art
On considering beneficial matters of consideration
In forms of arguments
And it includes nine chapters:
The first chapter: On begging the first question.
The second chapter: On verifying the placing of what is not a cause for the false conclusion as being a cause for it.
The third chapter: On verifying what is promoted by each of the two disputants.
The fourth chapter: On how to deduce the true from the false.
The fifth chapter: On how to acquire premises and obtain syllogisms of the desired things.
The sixth chapter: On analyzing syllogisms and referring to specific matters and warnings in that.
The seventh chapter: On eliciting conclusions.
The eighth chapter: On how knowledge and opinion or ignorance of its opposite come together in one thing.
The ninth chapter: On verifying what occurs regarding the converse of conclusions, premises, concomitants, opposites, and other accidents due to the states of terms.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a transitional and introductory section. It completes the fourth article on defining the forms of arguments (suwar al-hijaj). The author first distinguishes between argument forms that are useful for certainty (yaqin) and those that are not. Those not useful for certainty are not a source of error (ghalat) in dialectic (jadal) and rhetoric (khataba) because their purpose is persuasion based on common opinions (mashhur), not certainty. Then the author introduces a new 'art' (fann) — the eighth — which is about beneficial considerations regarding argument forms. This art contains nine chapters, each dealing with a specific logical or dialectical issue: begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub), false cause (placing something as a cause that is not), tactics in disputation, deriving truth from falsehood, acquiring premises, analyzing syllogisms, eliciting conclusions, combining knowledge with opinion or ignorance, and the effects of changes in terms on conversions and oppositions. This structure sets up the detailed discussions that follow. The student should note that this is a methodological outline, not a set of definitions or proofs.

اليقين
الغلط
الجدل
الخطابة
المشهور

D) To combine knowledge and ignorance in one proposition.

Key Takeaway
The eighth art distinguishes argument forms used for certainty (burhan) from those used for persuasion (jadal/khataba), emphasizing that contexts determine validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given this argument from a kalam text: 'The world is temporally originated; everything temporally originated has a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Determine whether this argument is meant for certainty (demonstration) or for dialectical persuasion, and justify based on the passage's criteria.

Overview of the Eighth Art's Nine Chapters
Author lists the nine chapters of the eighth art on beneficial considerations in argument forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the nine chapters of the eighth art and classify each by its primary logical or dialectical concern.

Core Idea
The nine chapters progress from foundational fallacies to advanced techniques in argument analysis and disputation.

After establishing the purpose of the eighth art, the author lists nine chapters that form its content. These chapters cover a range of topics: from classic fallacies like begging the question, to methods for deriving truth from falsehood, to the interplay of knowledge and opinion. This lesson will help you parse this list and understand how each chapter contributes to the art of argument evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the nine chapters in the text
Find the section in the Arabic that begins with 'ويشتمل على تسعة فصول' and ends with the last chapter. Read through the titles of all nine chapters.
The chapters are numbered from الفصل الأول to الفصل التاسع.
2
Group chapters by thematic focus
Identify which chapters deal with fallacies (e.g., begging the question, false cause), which deal with method (e.g., acquiring premises, analyzing syllogisms), and which deal with special topics (e.g., knowledge and opinion together). Write a short label for each chapter.
Fallacies: Ch.1 (musadara), Ch.2 (false cause). Method: Ch.5,6,7. Special: Ch.4,8,9. Ch.3 seems dialectical.
3
Reflect on the sequence
Notice that the early chapters focus on errors and misuse (fallacies), then move to constructive methods (acquiring premises, analysis), and finally to more advanced and subtle issues (combination of knowledge and opinion, effects of term changes). Why might this order be pedagogically effective?

Continuation of the fourth article on the definition of forms of arguments.
Some forms of arguments here are not useful for certainty, and it is something of error that leads to error in demonstration, but it is not a place for error in dialectic and rhetoric; for the purpose of it is only the binding in the well-known by what is usually thought, and it is not falling short of that.
Cause
The eighth art
On considering beneficial matters of consideration
In forms of arguments
And it includes nine chapters:
The first chapter: On begging the first question.
The second chapter: On verifying the placing of what is not a cause for the false conclusion as being a cause for it.
The third chapter: On verifying what is promoted by each of the two disputants.
The fourth chapter: On how to deduce the true from the false.
The fifth chapter: On how to acquire premises and obtain syllogisms of the desired things.
The sixth chapter: On analyzing syllogisms and referring to specific matters and warnings in that.
The seventh chapter: On eliciting conclusions.
The eighth chapter: On how knowledge and opinion or ignorance of its opposite come together in one thing.
The ninth chapter: On verifying what occurs regarding the converse of conclusions, premises, concomitants, opposites, and other accidents due to the states of terms.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a transitional and introductory section. It completes the fourth article on defining the forms of arguments (suwar al-hijaj). The author first distinguishes between argument forms that are useful for certainty (yaqin) and those that are not. Those not useful for certainty are not a source of error (ghalat) in dialectic (jadal) and rhetoric (khataba) because their purpose is persuasion based on common opinions (mashhur), not certainty. Then the author introduces a new 'art' (fann) — the eighth — which is about beneficial considerations regarding argument forms. This art contains nine chapters, each dealing with a specific logical or dialectical issue: begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub), false cause (placing something as a cause that is not), tactics in disputation, deriving truth from falsehood, acquiring premises, analyzing syllogisms, eliciting conclusions, combining knowledge with opinion or ignorance, and the effects of changes in terms on conversions and oppositions. This structure sets up the detailed discussions that follow. The student should note that this is a methodological outline, not a set of definitions or proofs.

The Ninth Art: Nine Chapters of Useful Argument Forms
Author lists the nine chapters of the eighth art of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the nine chapters of the eighth art and categorize them into logical fallacies, dialectical techniques, and analysis methods.

Core Idea
The eighth art is a toolkit for spotting fallacies and mastering dialectical strategy.

After mastering the construction of syllogisms in the earlier arts, the eighth art turns to the pitfalls and advanced manoeuvres of argumentation. This passage provides a roadmap of nine chapters, each addressing a distinct logical or dialectical issue. Understanding this structure will help you navigate the detailed discussions that follow and apply them in real debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the art's purpose
The title says 'useful considerations regarding the forms of arguments.' This art is not about valid forms but about problems and tactics in argumentation.
2
List the nine chapters
Read through the Arabic list and note each chapter's topic. Chapters 1-2 deal with fallacies (begging the question and false cause). Chapters 3-4 and 7-8 cover dialectical moves and paradoxes. Chapters 5-6 and 9 focus on analysis and conversion.
Memorize the order: 1. Begging the question, 2. False cause, 3. Promoting objections, 4. Deduce truth from falsehood, 5. Acquire premises, 6. Analyze syllogisms, 7. Provoking conclusions, 8. Coexistence of knowledge and opinion, 9. Conversion of propositions.
3
Group chapters by theme
Ch.1-2: fallacies. Ch.3-4,7-8: dialectical techniques. Ch.5-6,9: how to construct, analyze, and transform arguments. This grouping helps you recall the scope.
You may create your own categories; the key is to see that the art covers both fallacy detection and strategic reasoning.
4
Relate to Islamic debate (munazara)
In a classical Islamic debate, a disputant may commit begging the question (مصادرة) or falsely attribute a cause. The tools in these chapters help the judge identify such errors and evaluate the strength of arguments.

The Eighth Art: On Considering Useful Matters Regarding the Forms of Arguments. It comprises nine chapters: Chapter One: On Begging the Question. Chapter Two: On Investigating the Posing of What is Not a Cause for a False Conclusion as if it Were a Cause for It. Chapter Three: On Investigating What is Promoted by the Objection of Each of the Two Disputants. Chapter Four: On How to Deduce the True from the False. Commentary
This passage introduces the eighth art of logic, which deals with useful considerations regarding the forms of arguments. It functions as a table of contents, listing nine chapters that cover various logical fallacies and dialectical techniques. The first chapter treats begging the question (مصادرة على المطلوب الأول). The second discusses the fallacy of false cause (وضع ما ليس سبباً للنتيجة الكاذبة على أنه لها). The third examines how disputants promote objections. The fourth explains deducing truth from falsehood. The fifth covers acquiring premises and constructing syllogisms. The sixth focuses on analyzing syllogisms with helpful hints. The seventh is about provoking conclusions. The eighth explores how knowledge, opinion, and ignorance can coexist regarding the same thing. The ninth concerns the conversion of propositions and other accidents due to definitions. This structure shows that the eighth art is concerned with both formal and informal aspects of argumentation.

The Twelve Chapters of the Fifth Article on Burhan
Author enumerates the twelve chapters of the fifth article on burhan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the twelve chapters of the fifth article on burhan and identify how each contributes to the science of demonstration.

Core Idea
The structure of burhan reveals the logical anatomy of certain knowledge.

The fifth article of this work is devoted entirely to the science of demonstration (burhan). Before diving into the details, the author provides an outline of its twenty-two chapters. Though only the first twelve are listed here, this outline is not merely a table of contents—it is a carefully arranged sequence that moves from definitions to materials, conditions, errors, and the relationships among sciences. Understanding this progression will help you see how demonstration is built step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall topic
The article is on burhan (demonstration). The outline starts with a definition and purpose.
Chapter one defines demonstration and its rank among the sciences.
2
Group the chapters by theme
Look for clusters: chapters 1-2 define and list materials; chapters 3-4 deal with demands and the definition of demonstration; chapters 5-9 discuss epistemological conditions and errors; chapters 10-12 concern the sciences themselves.
This grouping helps understand the logical flow of the article.
3
Trace the logical progression
The outline moves from the essence of demonstration to its premises, then to their conditions, and finally to how demonstration relates to different sciences.
Notice the increasing specificity: from general definition to particular conditions and applications.
4
Memorize the first twelve chapters
Use the Arabic titles as anchor points: الفصل الأول في عرضه ومقصوده, etc. Practice reciting them in order.
Memorization aids recall when studying individual chapters later.

The fifth article on the science of demonstration. It contains twenty-two chapters. Chapter one: on its definition, purpose, and verifying its rank. Chapter two: on the materials of syllogisms, enumerating their types, and what is the material of each type. Chapter three: on the demands (al-matalib), the quantity of their divisions, and the manner of their ordering. Chapter four: on demonstration. Chapter five: on how to know the primary propositions that have no cause, and how the more specific is the cause of the more general. Chapter six: on the conditions of the premises of demonstration, verifying its principle, and what pertains to it regarding necessity and universality. Chapter seven: on the essential predicates that are conditions in demonstration. Chapter eight: on the meaning of 'primary' (al-awwali). Chapter nine: on the causes of error in giving the dictum de omni in the book of demonstration, and verifying the necessity of its premises and their appropriateness. Chapter ten: on the subjects of the sciences, their principles, and their problems. Chapter eleven: on the differentiation and commonality of the sciences. Chapter twelve: on how to transfer demonstration from one science to another and how to handle it.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the fifth article (maqala) of the work, which is dedicated to the science of demonstration (ilm al-burhan). The author states that this article contains twenty-two chapters (fusul), but only the first twelve are listed on these pages. The list begins with a chapter defining demonstration and its purpose, then proceeds to the materials of syllogisms, the types of demands (matalib), and the nature of demonstration itself. Later chapters cover epistemological topics such as how primary propositions are known, the conditions for demonstrative premises, essential predicates, and the meaning of 'primary.' The author also addresses common errors in reasoning, the subjects and principles of sciences, the relationships between sciences, and the transfer of demonstration from one science to another. This outline reveals a systematic approach to the study of logical demonstration, typical of post-classical Islamic logic texts.

Overview of the Science of Demonstration: 22 Chapters
Lists the 22 chapters of the fifth article on demonstration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the 22 chapters of the fifth article on demonstration and describe the main topic of each.

Core Idea
The 22 chapters map the entire science of demonstration from premises to definitions.

This lesson introduces the table of contents for the fifth article of Daqā’iq al-Ḥaqā’iq, which is devoted entirely to the science of demonstration (burhān). Knowing this roadmap helps you navigate the detailed discussions ahead, from the purpose of demonstration to the relationship between definition and proof. As a memorization anchor, this overview will guide your study of each chapter.

Guided Path
1
Survey the overall structure
Read the list of 22 chapters quickly to see the breadth of topics covered: from the purpose of demonstration, through premises, to definitions and causes.
2
Group chapters into thematic sections
Identify clusters: Chapters 1-4 introduce demonstration and its materials; 5-9 deal with premises and primaries; 10-13 cover sciences and transfer of demonstration; 14-17 discuss fallacies, senses, and superiority; 18-22 treat cognitive terms and the definition-demonstration link.
This grouping aids memorization.
3
Highlight core chapters on demonstration itself
Focus on Chapter 4 (On demonstration), Chapter 12 (Transfer of demonstration), and Chapter 13 (Types: burhān al-lim and burhān al-anna) as central to the article's main subject.
4
Memorize the sequence using a mnemonic
Create a mnemonic from the first Arabic word of each chapter (e.g., عرض, مواد, مطالب, برهان, etc.) or associate numbers with key terms.
Example: 1-‘Ard, 2-Mawād, 3-Maṭālib, 4-Burhān...
5
Self-test: Recite chapter titles in order
Without looking, try to list all 22 chapters. Check your list against the source and note any gaps or errors.
Repeat until you can recall the entire sequence.
